Parsing [sent. 1 len. 11]: The Children of the New Forest , by Captain Marryat .
Parsing [sent. 2 len. 15]: Captain Frederick Marryat was born July 10 1792 , and died August 8 1848 .
Parsing [sent. 3 len. 16]: He retired from the British navy in 1828 in order to devote himself to writing .
Parsing [sent. 4 len. 31]: In the following 20 years he wrote 26 books , many of which are among the very best of English literature , and some of which are still in print .
Parsing [sent. 5 len. 14]: Marryat had an extraordinary gift for the invention of episodes in his stories .
Parsing [sent. 6 len. 24]: He says somewhere that when he sat down for the day 's work , he never knew what he was going to write .
Parsing [sent. 7 len. 7]: He certainly was a literary genius .
Parsing [sent. 8 len. 33]: `` The Children of the New Forest '' was published in 1847 , the twenty-fourth book to flow from Marryat 's pen , and the last published whilst he was still alive .
Parsing [sent. 9 len. 22]: It was written for children , and has been phenomenally succesful : it is still in print over 150 years later .
Parsing [sent. 10 len. 21]: This e-text was transcribed in 1998 by Nick Hodson , and was reformatted in 2003 , and again in 2005 .
Parsing [sent. 11 len. 12]: THE CHILDREN OF THE NEW FOREST , BY CAPTAIN FREDERICK MARRYAT .
Parsing [sent. 12 len. 3]: CHAPTER ONE .
Parsing [sent. 13 len. 19]: The circumstances which I am about to relate to my juvenile readers took place in the year 1647 .
Parsing [sent. 14 len. 51]: By referring to the history of England of that date they will find that King Charles the First , against whom the Commons of England had rebelled , after a civil war of nearly five years , had been defeated , and was confined as a prisoner at Hampton Court .
Parsing [sent. 15 len. 33]: The Cavaliers , or the party who fought for King Charles , had all been dispersed , and the Parliamentary army under the command of Cromwell were beginning to control the Commons .
Parsing [sent. 16 len. 52]: It was in the month of November in this year that King Charles , accompanied by Sir John Berkely Ashburnham and Legg , made his escape from Hampton Court , and rode as fast as the horses could carry them towards that part of Hampshire which led to the New Forest .
Parsing [sent. 17 len. 25]: The king expected that his friends had provided a vessel in which he might escape to France ; but in this he was disappointed .
Parsing [sent. 18 len. 31]: There was no vessel ready , and after riding for some time along the shore he resolved to go to Titchfield , a seat belonging to the Earl of Southampton .
Parsing [sent. 19 len. 48]: After a long consultation with those who attended him , he yielded to their advice , which was , to trust to Colonel Hammond , who was governor of the Isle of Wight for the Parliament , but who was supposed to be friendly to the king .
Parsing [sent. 20 len. 43]: Whatever might be the feelings of commiseration of Colonel Hammond towards a king so unfortunately situated , he was firm in his duties towards his employers , and the consequence was that King Charles found himself again a prisoner in Carisbrook Castle .
Parsing [sent. 21 len. 19]: But we must now leave the king , and retrace history to the commencement of the civil war .
Parsing [sent. 22 len. 61]: A short distance from the town of Lymington , which is not far from Titchfield , where the king took shelter , but on the other side of the Southampton Water , and south of the New Forest , to which it adjoins , was a property called Arnwood , which belonged to a Cavalier of the name of Beverley .
Parsing [sent. 23 len. 44]: It was at that time a property of considerable value , being very extensive , and the park ornamented with valuable timber ; for it abutted on the New Forest , and might have been supposed to have been a continuation of it .
Parsing [sent. 24 len. 40]: This Colonel Beverley , as we must call him , for he rose to that rank in the king 's army , was a valued friend and companion of Prince Rupert 's , and commanded several troops of cavalry .
Parsing [sent. 25 len. 29]: He was ever at his side in the brilliant charges made by this gallant prince , and at last fell in his arms at the battle of Naseby .
Parsing [sent. 26 len. 61]: Colonel Beverley had married into the family of the Villiers , and the issue of his marriage was two sons and two daughters ; but his zeal and sense of duty had induced him , at the commencement of the war , to leave his wife and family at Arnwood , and he was fated never to meet them again .
Parsing [sent. 27 len. 77]: The news of his death had such an effect upon Mrs Beverley , already worn with anxiety on her husband 's account , that a few months afterwards she followed him to an early tomb , leaving the four children under the charge of an elderly relative till such time as the family of the Villiers could protect them ; but , as will appear by our history , this was not at that period possible .
Parsing [sent. 28 len. 37]: The life of a king and many other lives were in jeopardy , and the orphans remained at Arnwood , still under the care of their elderly relation , at the time that our history commences .
Parsing [sent. 29 len. 70]: The New Forest , my readers are perhaps aware , was first enclosed by William the Conqueror as a royal forest for his own amusement , for in those days most crowned heads were passionately fond of the chase ; and they may also recollect that his successor , William Rufus , met his death in this forest by the glancing of an arrow shot by Sir Walter Tyrrell .
Parsing [sent. 30 len. 14]: Since that time to the present day it has continued a royal domain .
Parsing [sent. 31 len. 30]: At the period of which we are writing it had an establishment of verderers and keepers , paid by the Crown , amounting to some forty or fifty men .
Parsing [sent. 32 len. 86]: At the commencement of the civil war they remained at their posts , but soon found , in the disorganised state of the country , that their wages were no longer to be obtained ; and then , when the king had decided upon raising an army , Beverley , who held a superior office in the forest , enrolled all the young and athletic men who were employed in the forest , and marched them away with him to join the king 's army .
Parsing [sent. 33 len. 52]: Some few remained , their age not rendering their services of value , and among them was an old and attached servant of Beverley 's , a man above sixty years of age , whose name was Jacob Armitage , and who had obtained the situation through Colonel Beverley 's interest .
Parsing [sent. 34 len. 34]: Those who remained in the forest lived in cottages many miles asunder , and indemnified themselves for the non-payment of their salaries by killing the deer for sale and for their own subsistence .
Parsing [sent. 35 len. 96]: The cottage of Jacob Armitage was situated on the skirts of the New Forest , about a mile and a half from the mansion of Arnwood ; and when Colonel Beverley went to join the king 's troops , feeling how little security there would be for his wife and children in those troubled times , he requested the old man , by his attachment to the family , not to lose sight of Arnwood , but to call there as often as possible to see if he could be of service to Mrs Beverley .
Parsing [sent. 36 len. 25]: The colonel would have persuaded Jacob to have altogether taken up his residence at the mansion ; but to this the old man objected .
Parsing [sent. 37 len. 20]: He had been all his life under the greenwood tree , and could not bear to leave the forest .
Parsing [sent. 38 len. 26]: He promised the colonel that he would watch over his family , and ever be at hand when required ; and he kept his word .
Parsing [sent. 39 len. 60]: The death of Colonel Beverley was a heavy blow to the old forester , and he watched over Mrs Beverley and the orphans with the greatest solicitude ; but when Mrs Beverley followed her husband to the tomb he then redoubled his attentions , and was seldom more than a few hours at a time away from the mansion .
Parsing [sent. 40 len. 27]: The two boys were his inseparable companions , and he instructed them , young as they were , in all the secrets of his own calling .
Parsing [sent. 41 len. 33]: Such was the state of affairs at the time that King Charles made his escape from Hampton Court ; and I now shall resume my narrative from where it was broken off .
Parsing [sent. 42 len. 45]: As soon as the escape of Charles the First was made known to Cromwell and the Parliament , troops of horse were despatched in every direction to the southward , towards which the prints of the horses ' hoofs proved that he had gone .
Parsing [sent. 43 len. 61]: As they found that he had proceeded in the direction of the New Forest , the troops were subdivided and ordered to scour the forest , in parties of twelve to twenty , while others hastened down to Southampton , Lymington , and every other seaport or part of the coast from which the king might be likely to embark .
Parsing [sent. 44 len. 64]: Old Jacob had been at Arnwood on the day before , but on this day he had made up his mind to procure some venison , that he might not go there again empty-handed ; for Miss Judith Villiers was very partial to venison , and was not slow to remind Jacob if the larder was for many days deficient in that meat .
Parsing [sent. 45 len. 68]: Jacob had gone out accordingly ; he had gained his leeward position of a fine buck , and was gradually nearing him by stealth , now behind a huge oak-tree , and then crawling through the high fern , so as to get within shot unperceived , when on a sudden the animal , which had been quietly feeding , bounded away and disappeared in the thicket .
Parsing [sent. 46 len. 23]: At the same time Jacob perceived a small body of horse galloping through the glen in which the buck had been feeding .
Parsing [sent. 47 len. 61]: Jacob had never yet seen the Parliamentary troops , for they had not during the war been sent into that part of the country , but their iron skull-caps , their buff accoutrements , and dark habiliments , assured him that such these must be ; so very different were they from the gaily-equipped Cavalier cavalry commanded by Prince Rupert .
Parsing [sent. 48 len. 81]: At the time that they advanced , Jacob had been lying down in the fern near to some low black-thorn-bushes ; not wishing to be perceived by them , he drew back between the bushes , intending to remain concealed until they should gallop out of sight ; for Jacob thought , `` I am a king 's forester , and they may consider me as an enemy ; and who knows how I may be treated by them ? ''
Parsing [sent. 49 len. 88]: But Jacob was disappointed in his expectations of the troops riding past him ; on the contrary , as soon as they arrived at an oak-tree within twenty yards of where he was concealed , the order was given to halt and dismount ; the sabres of the horsemen clattered in their iron sheaths as the order was obeyed , and the old man expected to be immediately discovered ; but one of the thorn-bushes was directly between him and the troopers , and effectually concealed him .
Parsing [sent. 50 len. 42]: At last Jacob ventured to raise his head and peep through the bush ; and he perceived that the men were loosening the girths of their black horses , or wiping away the perspiration from their sides with handfuls of fern .
Parsing [sent. 51 len. 40]: A powerfully-framed man , who appeared to command the others , was standing with his hand upon the arched neck of his steed , which appeared as fresh and vigorous as ever , although covered with foam and perspiration .
Parsing [sent. 52 len. 34]: `` Spare not to rub down , my men , '' said he , `` for we have tried the mettle of our horses , and have now but one half-hour 's breathing-time .
Parsing [sent. 53 len. 16]: We must be on , for the work of the Lord must be done . ''
Parsing [sent. 54 len. 66]: `` They say that this forest is many miles in length and breadth , '' observed another of the men , `` and we may ride many a mile to no purpose ; but here is James Southwold , who once was living in it as a verderer ; nay , I think that he said that he was born and bred in these woods .
Parsing [sent. 55 len. 9]: Was it not so , James Southwold ? ''
Parsing [sent. 56 len. 35]: `` It is even as you say , '' replied an active-looking young man ; `` I was born and bred in this forest , and my father was a verderer before me . ''
Parsing [sent. 57 len. 17]: Jacob Armitage , who listened to the conversation , immediately recognised the young man in question .
Parsing [sent. 58 len. 19]: He was one of those who had joined the king 's army with the other verderers and keepers .
Parsing [sent. 59 len. 101]: It pained him much to perceive that one who had always been considered a frank , true-hearted young man , and who left the forest to fight in defence of his king , was now turned a traitor , and had joined the ranks of the enemy ; and Jacob thought how much better it had been for James Southwold if he had never quitted the New Forest , and had not been corrupted by evil company : `` He was a good lad , '' thought Jacob , `` and now he is a traitor and a hypocrite . ''
Parsing [sent. 60 len. 31]: `` If born and bred in this forest , James Southwold , '' said the leader of the troop , `` you must fain know all its mazes and paths .
Parsing [sent. 61 len. 27]: Now call to mind , are there no secret hiding-places in which people may remain concealed ; no thickets which may cover both man and horse ?
Parsing [sent. 62 len. 17]: Peradventure thou mayst point out the very spot where this man Charles may be hidden . ''
Parsing [sent. 63 len. 34]: `` I do know one dell , within a mile of Arnwood , '' replied James Southwold , `` which might cover double our troop from the eyes of the most wary . ''
Parsing [sent. 64 len. 13]: `` We will ride there , then , '' replied the leader .
Parsing [sent. 65 len. 6]: `` Arnwood , sayest thou ?
Parsing [sent. 66 len. 20]: Is not that the property of the Malignant , Cavalier Beverley , who was shot down at Naseby ? ''
Parsing [sent. 67 len. 52]: `` Even so , '' replied Southwold ; `` and many is the time -- that is , in the olden time , before I was regenerated -- many is the day of revelry that I have passed there ; many the cup of good ale that I have quaffed . ''
Parsing [sent. 68 len. 13]: `` And thou shalt quaff it again , '' replied the leader .
Parsing [sent. 69 len. 17]: `` Good ale was not intended only for Malignants , but for those who serve diligently .

Parsing [sent. 74 len. 21]: `` I have searched many of their abodes , '' observed another ; `` but search is almost in vain .

Parsing [sent. 76 len. 48]: `` Yes , '' replied the leader , `` their abodes are full of these Popish abominations ; but there is one way which is sure ; and if the man Charles be concealed in any house , I venture to say that I will find him .
Parsing [sent. 77 len. 43]: Fire and smoke will bring him forth ; and to every Malignant 's house within twenty miles will I apply the torch ; but it must be at night , for we are not sure of his being housed during the day .
Parsing [sent. 78 len. 12]: James Southwold , thou knowest well the mansion of Arnwood ? ''

Parsing [sent. 99 len. 55]: As perhaps my readers may ask the same question , they must know that a large proportion of the Parliamentary army had at this time assumed the name of Levellers , in consequence of having taken up the opinion that every man should be on an equality , and property should be equally divided .
Parsing [sent. 100 len. 64]: The hatred of these people to any one above them in rank or property , especially towards those of the king 's party , which mostly consisted of men of rank and property , was unbounded , and they were merciless and cruel to the highest degree ; throwing off much of hat fanatical bearing and language which had before distinguished the Puritans .

Parsing [sent. 111 len. 23]: Before Jacob is admitted to the presence of Miss Judith Villiers , we must give some account of the establishment at Arnwood .
Parsing [sent. 112 len. 55]: With the exception of one male servant , who officiated in the house and stable as his services might be required , every man of the household of Colonel Beverley had followed the fortunes of their master , and as none had returned , they , in all probability , had shared his fate .
Parsing [sent. 113 len. 15]: Three female servants , with the man above mentioned , composed the whole household .
Parsing [sent. 114 len. 26]: Indeed , there was every reason for not increasing the establishment ; for the rents were either paid in part or not paid at all .
Parsing [sent. 115 len. 59]: It was generally supposed that the property , now that the Parliament had gained the day , would be sequestrated , although such was not yet the case ; and the tenants were unwilling to pay , to those who were not authorised to receive , the rents which they might be again called upon to make good .
Parsing [sent. 116 len. 54]: Miss Judith Villiers , therefore , found it difficult to maintain the present household ; and although she did not tell Jacob Armitage that such was the case , the fact was , that very often the venison which he brought to the mansion was all the meat that was in the larder .
Parsing [sent. 117 len. 35]: The three female servants held the offices of cook , attendant upon Miss Villiers , and housemaid ; the children being under the care of no particular servant , and left much to themselves .
Parsing [sent. 118 len. 100]: There had been a chaplain in the house , but he had quitted before the death of Mrs Beverley , and the vacancy had not been filled up ; indeed , it could not well be , for the one who left had not received his salary for many months , and Miss Judith Villiers , expecting every day to be summoned by her relations to bring the children and join them , sat in her high chair waiting for the arrival of this summons , which , from the distracted state of the times , had never come .
Parsing [sent. 119 len. 25]: As we have before said , the orphans were four in number ; the two eldest were boys , and the youngest were girls .

Parsing [sent. 125 len. 27]: The other two had more than sufficient employment ; as , when there is no money to pay with , everything must be done at home .

Parsing [sent. 498 len. 21]: There were no more visits to the cottage that day , and they all went to bed and slept soundly .
Parsing [sent. 499 len. 38]: The next morning Jacob , who was most anxious to learn the news , saddled the pony , having first given his injunctions to Edward how to behave in case any troopers should come to the cottage .
Parsing [sent. 500 len. 23]: He told him to pretend that the children were in bed with the smallpox , as they had done the day before .
Parsing [sent. 501 len. 44]: Jacob then travelled to Gossip Allwood 's , and he there learnt that King Charles had been taken prisoner , and was at the Isle of Wight , and that the troopers were all going back to London as fast as they came .
Parsing [sent. 502 len. 25]: Feeling that there was now no more danger to be apprehended from them , Jacob set off as fast as he could for Lymington .
Parsing [sent. 503 len. 31]: He went to one shop and purchased two peasant dresses which he thought would fit the two boys , and at another he bought similar apparel for the two girls .
Parsing [sent. 504 len. 66]: Then with several other ready-made articles , and some other things which were required for the household , he made a large package , which he put upon the pony , and taking the bridle , set off home , and arrived in time to superintend the cooking of the dinner , which was this day venison-steaks fried in a pan , and boiled potatoes .
Parsing [sent. 505 len. 56]: When dinner was over he opened his bundle , and told the little ones that now they were to live in a cottage they ought to wear cottage clothes , and that he had brought them some to put on , which they might rove about the woods in , and not mind tearing them .

Parsing [sent. 508 len. 35]: `` Now , recollect , you are all my grandchildren , '' said Jacob ; `` for I shall no longer call you Miss and Master -- that we never do in a cottage .

Parsing [sent. 510 len. 3]: added Jacob .

Parsing [sent. 512 len. 20]: We must now describe the cottage of Jacob Armitage , in which the children have in future to dwell .
Parsing [sent. 513 len. 74]: As we said before , it contained a large sitting-room , or kitchen , in which were a spacious hearth and chimney , table , stools , cupboards , and dressers ; the two bedrooms which adjoined it were now appropriated , one for Jacob and the other for the two boys ; the third , or inner bedroom , was arranged for the two girls , as being more retired and secure .
Parsing [sent. 514 len. 97]: But there were outhouses belonging to it : a stall , in which White Billy , the pony , lived during the winter ; a shed and pigsty rudely constructed , with an enclosed yard attached to them ; and it had , moreover , a piece of ground of more than an acre , well fenced in to keep out the deer and game , the largest portion of which was cultivated as a garden and potato-ground , and the other , which remained in grass , contained some fine old apple and pear trees .
Parsing [sent. 515 len. 31]: Such was the domicile ; the pony , a few fowls , a sow and two young pigs , and the dog Smoker , were the animals on the establishment .
Parsing [sent. 516 len. 27]: Here Jacob Armitage had been born -- for the cottage had been built by his grandfather -- but he had not always remained at the cottage .
Parsing [sent. 517 len. 24]: When young , he felt an inclination to see more of the world , and had for several years served in the army .
Parsing [sent. 518 len. 21]: His father and brother had lived in the establishment at Arnwood , and he was constantly there as a boy .
Parsing [sent. 519 len. 23]: The chaplain of Arnwood had taken a fancy to him , and taught him to read -- writing he had not acquired .
Parsing [sent. 520 len. 61]: As soon as he grew up he served , as we have said , in the troop commanded by Colonel Beverley 's father ; and after his death , Colonel Beverley had procured him the situation of forest ranger , which had been held by his father , who was then alive , but too aged to do the duty .
Parsing [sent. 521 len. 52]: Jacob Armitage married a good and devout young woman , with whom he lived several years , when she died , without bringing him any family ; after which , his father being also dead , Jacob Armitage had lived alone until the period at which we have commenced this history .
Parsing [sent. 522 len. 3]: CHAPTER FOUR .
Parsing [sent. 523 len. 48]: The old forester lay awake the whole of this night , reflecting how he should act relative to the children ; he felt the great responsibility that he had incurred , and was alarmed when he considered what might be the consequences if his days were shortened .
Parsing [sent. 524 len. 34]: What would become of them -- living in so sequestered a spot that few knew even of its existence -- totally shut out from the world , and left to their own resources ?
Parsing [sent. 525 len. 40]: He had no fear , if his life was spared , that they would do well ; but if he should be called away before they had grown up and were able to help themselves , they might perish .
Parsing [sent. 526 len. 40]: Edward was not fourteen years old ; it was true that he was an active , brave boy , and thoughtful for his years ; but he had not yet strength or skill sufficient for what would be required .
Parsing [sent. 527 len. 16]: Humphrey , the second , also promised well ; but still they were all children .
Parsing [sent. 528 len. 67]: `` I must bring them up to be useful -- to depend upon themselves ; there is not a moment to be lost , and not a moment shall be lost ; I will do my best , and trust to God ; I ask but two or three years , and by that time I trust that they will be able to do without me .
Parsing [sent. 529 len. 12]: They must commence to-morrow the life of foresters ' children . ''
Parsing [sent. 530 len. 82]: Acting upon this resolution , Jacob , as soon as the children were dressed and in the sitting-room , opened his Bible , which he had put on the table , and said : `` My dear children , you know that you must remain in this cottage , that the wicked troopers may not find you out ; they killed your father , and if I had not taken you away , they would have burnt you in your beds .
Parsing [sent. 531 len. 73]: You must therefore live here as my children , and you must call yourselves by the name of Armitage , and not that of Beverley ; and you must dress like children of the forest , as you do now , and you must do as children of the forest do ; that is , you must do everything for yourselves , for you can have no servants to wait upon you .
Parsing [sent. 532 len. 27]: We must all work ; but you will like to work if you all work together , for then the work will be nothing but play .
Parsing [sent. 533 len. 51]: Now , Edward is the oldest , and he must go out with me in the forest , and I must teach him to kill deer and other game for our support ; and when he knows how , then Humphrey shall come out and learn how to shoot . ''
Parsing [sent. 534 len. 14]: `` Yes , '' said Humphrey , `` I 'll soon learn . ''
Parsing [sent. 535 len. 72]: `` But not yet , Humphrey , for you must do some work in the meantime ; you must look after the pony and the pigs , and you must learn to dig in the garden with Edward and me when we do not go out to hunt ; and sometimes I shall go by myself , and leave Edward to work with you when there is work to be done .
Parsing [sent. 536 len. 21]: Alice , dear , you must , with Humphrey , light the fire and clean the house in the morning .
Parsing [sent. 537 len. 55]: Humphrey will go to the spring for water , and do all the hard work ; and you must learn to wash , my dear Alice -- I will show you how ; and you must learn to get dinner ready with Humphrey , who will assist you ; and to make the beds .
Parsing [sent. 538 len. 28]: And little Edith shall take care of the fowls , and feed them every morning , and look for the eggs -- will you , Edith ? ''
Parsing [sent. 539 len. 26]: `` Yes , '' replied Edith , `` and feed all the little chickens when they are hatched , as I did at Arnwood . ''
Parsing [sent. 540 len. 12]: `` Yes , dear , and you 'll be very useful .
Parsing [sent. 541 len. 13]: Now you know that you can not do all this at once .
Parsing [sent. 542 len. 23]: You will have to try and try again ; but very soon you will , and then it will be all play .
Parsing [sent. 543 len. 23]: I must teach you all , and every day you will do it better , till you want no teaching at all .
Parsing [sent. 544 len. 22]: And now , my dear children , as there is no chaplain here , we must read the Bible every morning .
Parsing [sent. 545 len. 13]: Edward can read , I know ; can you , Humphrey ? ''
Parsing [sent. 546 len. 10]: `` Yes , all except the big words . ''
Parsing [sent. 547 len. 11]: `` Well , you will learn them by and by .

Parsing [sent. 549 len. 6]: It will be an amusement .
Parsing [sent. 550 len. 15]: Now tell me , do you all like what I have told you ? ''
Parsing [sent. 551 len. 32]: `` Yes , '' they all replied ; and then Jacob Armitage read a chapter in the Bible , after which they all knelt down and said the Lord 's Prayer .
Parsing [sent. 552 len. 17]: As this was done every morning and every evening , I need not repeat it again .
Parsing [sent. 553 len. 80]: Jacob then showed them again how to clean the house , and Humphrey and Alice soon finished their work under his directions ; and then they all sat down to breakfast , which was a very plain one , being generally cold meat , and cakes baked on the embers , at which Alice was soon very expert ; and little Edith was very useful in watching them for her , while she busied herself about her other work .
Parsing [sent. 554 len. 26]: But the venison was nearly all gone ; and after breakfast Jacob and Edward , with the dog Smoker , went out into the woods .
Parsing [sent. 555 len. 42]: Edward had no gun , as he only went out to be taught how to approach the game , which required great caution ; indeed Jacob had no second gun to give him , if he had wished so to do .
Parsing [sent. 556 len. 35]: `` Now , Edward , we are going after a fine stag , if we can find him -- which I doubt not -- but the difficulty is to get within shot of him .
Parsing [sent. 557 len. 42]: Recollect that you must always be hid , for his sight is very quick ; never be heard , for his ear is sharp ; and never come down to him with the wind , for his scent is very fine .
Parsing [sent. 558 len. 12]: Then you must hunt according to the hour of the day .
Parsing [sent. 559 len. 20]: At this time he is feeding ; two hours hence he will be lying down in the high fern .
Parsing [sent. 560 len. 20]: The dog is of no use unless the stag is badly wounded , when the dog will take him .
Parsing [sent. 561 len. 16]: Smoker knows his duty well , and will hide himself as close as we do .
Parsing [sent. 562 len. 55]: We are now going into the thick wood ahead of us , as there are many little spots of cleared ground in it where we may find the deer ; but we must keep more to the left , for the wind is to the eastward , and we must walk up against it .
Parsing [sent. 563 len. 32]: And now that we are coming into the wood , recollect , not a word must be said , and you must walk as quietly as possible , keeping behind me .
Parsing [sent. 564 len. 6]: Smoker , to heel ! ''
Parsing [sent. 565 len. 47]: They proceeded through the wood for more than a mile , when Jacob made a sign to Edward , and dropped down into the fern , crawling along to an open spot , where , at some distance , were a stag and three deer grazing .
Parsing [sent. 566 len. 34]: The deer grazed quietly , but the stag was ever and anon raising up his head and snuffing the air as he looked round , evidently acting as a sentinel for the females .
Parsing [sent. 567 len. 20]: The stag was perhaps a long quarter of a mile from where they had crouched down in the fern .
Parsing [sent. 568 len. 36]: Jacob remained immovable till the animal began to feed again , and then he advanced crawling through the fern , followed by Edward and the dog , who dragged himself on his stomach after Edward .
Parsing [sent. 569 len. 34]: This tedious approach was continued for some time , and they had neared the stag to within half the original distance , when the animal again lifted up his head and appeared uneasy .
Parsing [sent. 570 len. 7]: Jacob stopped and remained without motion .
Parsing [sent. 571 len. 45]: After a time the stag walked away , followed by the does , to the opposite side of the clear spot on which they had been feeding , and , to Edward 's annoyance , the animal was now half a mile from them .
Parsing [sent. 572 len. 40]: Jacob turned round and crawled into the wood , and when he knew that they were concealed he rose on his feet and said : `` You see , Edward , that it requires patience to stalk a deer .
Parsing [sent. 573 len. 5]: What a princely fellow !
Parsing [sent. 574 len. 14]: But he has probably been alarmed this morning , and is very uneasy .
Parsing [sent. 575 len. 23]: Now we must go through the woods till we come to the lee of him on the other side of the dell .
Parsing [sent. 576 len. 33]: You see he has led the does close to the thicket , and we shall have a better chance when we get there , if we are only quiet and cautious . ''
Parsing [sent. 577 len. 10]: `` What startled him , do you think ? ''
Parsing [sent. 578 len. 3]: said Edward .
Parsing [sent. 579 len. 31]: `` I think , when you were crawling through the fern after me , you broke a piece of rotten stick that was under you , did you not ? ''
Parsing [sent. 580 len. 11]: `` Yes , but that made but little noise . ''
Parsing [sent. 581 len. 24]: `` Quite enough to startle a red deer , Edward , as you will find out before you have been long a forester .
Parsing [sent. 582 len. 26]: These checks will happen , and have happened to me a hundred times , and then all the work is to be done over again .
Parsing [sent. 583 len. 15]: Now then to make the circuit -- we had better not say a word .
Parsing [sent. 584 len. 16]: If we get safe now to the other side we are sure of him . ''
Parsing [sent. 585 len. 25]: They proceeded at a quick walk through the forest , and in half an hour had gained the side where the deer were feeding .
Parsing [sent. 586 len. 72]: When about three hundred yards from the game , Jacob again sank down on his hands and knees , crawling from bush to bush , stopping whenever the stag raised his head , and advancing again when it resumed feeding ; at last they came to the fern at the side of the wood , and crawled through it as before , but still more cautiously as they approached the stag .
Parsing [sent. 587 len. 41]: In this manner they arrived at last to within eighty yards of the animal , and then Jacob advanced his gun ready to put it to his shoulder , and as he cocked the lock , raised himself to fire .
Parsing [sent. 588 len. 78]: The click occasioned by the cocking of the lock roused up the stag instantly , and he turned his head in the direction from whence the noise proceeded ; as he did so Jacob fired , aiming behind the animal 's shoulder : the stag made a bound , came down again , dropped on his knees , attempted to run , and fell dead , while the does fled away with the rapidity of the wind .
Parsing [sent. 589 len. 12]: Edward started up on his legs with a shout of exultation .
Parsing [sent. 590 len. 22]: Jacob commenced reloading his gun , and stopped Edward as he was about to run up to where the animal lay .
Parsing [sent. 591 len. 39]: `` Edward , you must learn your craft , '' said Jacob ; `` never do that again ; never shout in that way -- on the contrary , you should have remained still in the fern . ''
Parsing [sent. 592 len. 4]: `` Why so ?
Parsing [sent. 593 len. 6]: The stag is dead . ''
Parsing [sent. 594 len. 47]: `` Yes , my dear boy , that stag is dead ; but how do you know but what there may be another lying down in the fern close to us , or at some distance from us , which you have alarmed by your shout ?
Parsing [sent. 595 len. 65]: Suppose that we both had had guns , and that the report of mine had started another stag lying in the fern within shot , you would have been able to shoot it ; or if a stag was lying at a distance , the report of the gun might have startled him so as to induce him to move his head without rising .
Parsing [sent. 596 len. 27]: I should have seen his antlers move and have marked his lair , and we should then have gone after him and stalked him too . ''
Parsing [sent. 597 len. 22]: `` I see , '' replied Edward , `` I was wrong ; but I shall know better another time . ''
Parsing [sent. 598 len. 24]: `` That 's why I tell you , my boy , '' replied Jacob ; `` now let us go to our quarry .
Parsing [sent. 599 len. 10]: Ay , Edward , this is a noble beast .
Parsing [sent. 600 len. 15]: I thought that he was a hart royal , and so he is . ''
Parsing [sent. 601 len. 10]: `` What is a hart royal , Jacob ? ''
Parsing [sent. 602 len. 42]: `` Why , a stag is called a brocket until he is three years old ; at four years he is a staggart ; at five years a warrantable stag ; and after five years he becomes a hart royal . ''
Parsing [sent. 603 len. 10]: `` And how do you know his age ? ''
Parsing [sent. 604 len. 52]: `` By his antlers : you see that this stag has nine antlers ; now , a brocket has but two antlers , a staggart three , and a warrantable stag but four ; at six years old , the antlers increase in number until they sometimes have twenty or thirty .
Parsing [sent. 605 len. 15]: This is a fine beast , and the venison is now getting very good .
Parsing [sent. 606 len. 13]: Now you must see me do the work of my craft . ''
Parsing [sent. 607 len. 22]: Jacob then cut the throat of the animal , and afterwards cut off its head , and took out its bowels .
Parsing [sent. 608 len. 8]: `` Are you tired , Edward ? ''
Parsing [sent. 609 len. 15]: said Jacob , as he wiped his hunting-knife on the coat of the stag .
Parsing [sent. 610 len. 8]: `` No , not the least . ''
Parsing [sent. 611 len. 22]: `` Well , then , we are now , I should think , about four or five miles from the cottage .
Parsing [sent. 612 len. 7]: Could you find your way home ?
Parsing [sent. 613 len. 17]: But that is of no consequence , Smoker will lead you home by the shortest path .
Parsing [sent. 614 len. 25]: I will stay here , and you can saddle White Billy and come back with him , for he must carry the venison back .
Parsing [sent. 615 len. 23]: It 's more than we can manage -- indeed , as much as we can manage with White Billy to help us .
Parsing [sent. 616 len. 17]: There 's more than twenty stone of venison lying there , I can tell you . ''
Parsing [sent. 617 len. 26]: Edward immediately assented , and Jacob desiring Smoker to go home , set about flaying and cutting up the animal for its more convenient transportation .
Parsing [sent. 618 len. 29]: In an hour and a half Edward , attended by Smoker , returned with the pony , on whose back the chief portion of the venison was packed .
Parsing [sent. 619 len. 35]: Jacob took a large piece on his own shoulders , and Edward carried another , and Smoker , after regaling himself with a portion of the inside of the animal , came after them .
Parsing [sent. 620 len. 29]: During the walk home Jacob initiated Edward into the terms of venery and many other points connected with deer-stalking , with which we shall not trouble our readers .
Parsing [sent. 621 len. 39]: As soon as they arrived at the cottage the venison was hung up , the pony put in the stable , and then they sat down to dinner with an excellent appetite after their long morning 's walk .
Parsing [sent. 622 len. 36]: Alice and Humphrey had cooked the dinner themselves , and it was in the pot , smoking hot , when they returned ; and Jacob declared he never ate a better mess in his life .
Parsing [sent. 623 len. 22]: Alice was not a little proud of this , and of the praises she received from Edward and the old forester .
Parsing [sent. 624 len. 32]: The next day Jacob stated his intention of going to Lymington to dispose of a large portion of the venison , and bring back a sack of oatmeal for their cakes .
Parsing [sent. 625 len. 40]: Edward asked to accompany him , but Jacob replied : `` Edward , you must not think of showing yourself at Lymington , or anywhere else , for a long while , until you are grown out of memory .
Parsing [sent. 626 len. 25]: It would be folly , and you would risk your sisters ' and brother 's lives , perhaps , as well as your own .
Parsing [sent. 627 len. 26]: Never mention it again : the time will come when it will be necessary , perhaps ; if so , it can not be helped .
Parsing [sent. 628 len. 8]: At present you would be known immediately .
Parsing [sent. 629 len. 65]: No , Edward , I tell you what I do mean to do : I have a little money left , and I intend to buy you a gun , that you may learn to stalk deer yourself without me : for recollect , if any accident should happen to me , who is there but you to provide for your brother and sisters ?
Parsing [sent. 630 len. 51]: At Lymington I am known to many ; but out of all who know me , there is not one who knows where my cottage is ; they know that I live in the New Forest , and that I supply them venison , and purchase other articles in return .
Parsing [sent. 631 len. 15]: That is all that they know ; and I may therefore go without fear .
Parsing [sent. 632 len. 54]: I shall sell the venison to-morrow , and bring you back a good gun ; and Humphrey shall have the carpenters ' tools which he wishes for -- for I think , by what he does with his knife , that he has a turn that way , and it may be useful .
Parsing [sent. 633 len. 47]: I must also get some other tools for Humphrey and you , as we shall then be able to work all together ; and some threads and needles for Alice , for she can sew a little , and practice will make her more perfect . ''
Parsing [sent. 634 len. 69]: Jacob went off to Lymington as he had proposed , and returned late at night with White Billy well loaded ; he had a sack of oatmeal , some spades and hoes , a saw and chisels , and other tools ; two scythes and two three-pronged forks ; and when Edward came to meet him he put into his hand a gun with a very long barrel .
Parsing [sent. 635 len. 23]: `` I believe , Edward , that you will find that a good one , for I know where it came from .

Parsing [sent. 638 len. 13]: He was killed at Naseby , with your father , poor fellow !
Parsing [sent. 639 len. 12]: And his widow sold the gun to meet her wants . ''
Parsing [sent. 640 len. 4]: `` Well ! ''
Parsing [sent. 641 len. 38]: replied Edward , `` I thank you much , Jacob , and I will try if I can not kill as much venison as will pay back the purchase-money -- I will , I assure you . ''
Parsing [sent. 642 len. 40]: `` I shall be glad if you do , Edward ; not because I want the money back , but because then I shall be more easy in my mind about you all , if anything happens to me .
Parsing [sent. 643 len. 29]: As soon as you are perfect in your woodcraft , I shall take Humphrey in hand , for there is nothing like having two strings to your bow .

Parsing [sent. 645 len. 32]: You shall practise at a mark with your gun , that you may be accustomed to it : for all guns , even the best , require a little humouring . ''
Parsing [sent. 646 len. 42]: Edward , who had often fired a gun before , proved the next morning that he had a very good eye ; and after two or three hours ' practice , hit the mark at a hundred yards almost every time .
Parsing [sent. 647 len. 21]: `` I wish you would let me go out by myself , '' said Edward , overjoyed at his success .
Parsing [sent. 648 len. 13]: `` You would bring home nothing , boy , '' replied Jacob .
Parsing [sent. 649 len. 43]: `` No , no , you have a great deal to learn yet ; but I tell you what you shall do : any time that we are not in great want of venison , you shall have the first fire . ''
Parsing [sent. 650 len. 11]: `` Well , that will do , '' replied Edward .
Parsing [sent. 651 len. 17]: The winter now set in with great severity , and they remained almost altogether within doors .
Parsing [sent. 652 len. 18]: Jacob and the boys went out to get firewood , and dragged it home through the snow .
Parsing [sent. 653 len. 52]: `` I wish , Jacob , '' said Humphrey , `` that I was able to build a cart , for it would be very useful , and White Bill would then have something to do ; but I ca n't make the wheels , and there is no harness . ''
Parsing [sent. 654 len. 23]: `` That 's not a bad idea of yours , Humphrey , '' replied Jacob ; `` we will think about it .
Parsing [sent. 655 len. 14]: If you ca n't build a cart , perhaps I can buy one .

Parsing [sent. 659 len. 15]: But if they remained indoors during the inclement weather , they were not idle .
Parsing [sent. 660 len. 11]: Jacob took this opportunity to instruct the children in everything .
Parsing [sent. 661 len. 10]: Alice learnt how to wash and how to cook .
Parsing [sent. 662 len. 53]: It is true that sometimes she scalded herself a little , sometimes burnt her fingers ; and other accidents did occur , from the articles employed being too heavy for them to lift by themselves ; but practice and dexterity compensated for want of strength , and fewer accidents happened every day .
Parsing [sent. 663 len. 41]: Humphrey had his carpenters ' tools ; and although at first he had many failures , and wasted nails and wood , by degrees he learnt to use his tools with more dexterity , and made several little useful articles .

Parsing [sent. 665 len. 30]: It was astonishing how much the children could do , now that there was no one to do it for them ; and they had daily instruction from Jacob .
Parsing [sent. 666 len. 31]: In the evening Alice sat down with her needle and thread to mend the clothes ; at first they were not very well done ; but she improved every day .
Parsing [sent. 667 len. 49]: Edith and Humphrey learnt to read while Alice worked , and then Alice learnt ; and thus passed the winter away so rapidly , that although they had been five months at the cottage , it did not appear as if they had been there as many weeks .
Parsing [sent. 668 len. 43]: All were happy and contented , with the exception , perhaps , of Edward , who had fits of gloominess , and occasionally showed signs of impatience as to what was passing in the world , of which he remained in ignorance .
Parsing [sent. 669 len. 13]: That Edward Beverley had fits of gloominess and impatience is not surprising .
Parsing [sent. 670 len. 34]: Edward had been brought up as the heir of Arnwood ; and a boy at a very early age imbibes notions of his position , if it promises to be a high one .
Parsing [sent. 671 len. 15]: He was not two miles from that property which by right was his own .
Parsing [sent. 672 len. 26]: His own mansion had been reduced to ashes -- he himself was hidden in the forest , and he could not but feel his position .
Parsing [sent. 673 len. 32]: He sighed for the time when the king 's cause should be again triumphant , and his arrival at that age when he could in person support and uphold the cause .
Parsing [sent. 674 len. 39]: He longed to be in command as his father had been -- to lead his men on to victory -- to recover his property , and to revenge himself on those who had acted so cruelly towards him .
Parsing [sent. 675 len. 75]: This was human nature ; and much as Jacob Armitage would expostulate with him , and try to divert his feelings into other channels ; long as he would preach to him about forgiveness of injuries , and patience until better times should come , Edward could not help brooding over these thoughts , and if ever there was a breast animated with intense hatred against the Puritans it was that of Edward Beverley .
Parsing [sent. 676 len. 19]: Although this was to be lamented , it could not create surprise or wonder in the old forester .
Parsing [sent. 677 len. 42]: All he could do was , as much as possible to reason with him , to soothe his irritated feelings , and by constant employment try to make him forget for a time the feelings of ill-will which he had conceived .
Parsing [sent. 678 len. 94]: One thing was , however , sufficiently plain to Edward , which was , that whatever might be his wrongs , he had not the power at present to redress them ; and this feeling , perhaps , more than any other , held him in some sort of check ; and as the time when he might have an opportunity appeared far distant , even to his own sanguine imagination , so by degrees did he contrive to dismiss from his thoughts what it was no use to think about at present .
Parsing [sent. 679 len. 3]: CHAPTER FIVE .
Parsing [sent. 680 len. 34]: As we have before said , time passed rapidly ; with the exception of one or two excursions after venison , they remained in the cottage , and Jacob never went to Lymington .
Parsing [sent. 681 len. 19]: The frost had broken up , the snow had long disappeared , and the trees began to bud .
Parsing [sent. 682 len. 19]: The sun became powerful , and in the month of May the forest began again to look green .
Parsing [sent. 683 len. 52]: `` And now , Edward , '' said Jacob Armitage , one day at breakfast , `` we will try for venison again to sell at Lymington , for I must purchase Humphrey 's cart and harness ; so let us get our guns , and go out this fine morning .
Parsing [sent. 684 len. 19]: The stags are mostly by themselves at this season , for the does are with their young calves .
Parsing [sent. 685 len. 42]: We must find the slot of a deer , and track him to his lair , and you shall have the first shot if you like ; but that , however , depends more upon the deer than upon me . ''
Parsing [sent. 686 len. 43]: They had walked four or five miles when they came upon the slot or track of a deer , but Jacob 's practised eye pointed out to Edward that it was the slot of a young one , and not worth following .
Parsing [sent. 687 len. 54]: He explained to Edward the difference in the hoof-marks and other signs by which this knowledge was gained , and they proceeded onwards until they found another slot , which Jacob declared to be that of a warrantable stag -- that is , one old enough to kill and to be good venison .
Parsing [sent. 688 len. 13]: `` We must now track him to his lair , Edward . ''
Parsing [sent. 689 len. 23]: This took them about a mile farther , when they arrived at a small thicket of thorns about an acre in extent .
Parsing [sent. 690 len. 20]: `` Here he is , you see , Edward ; let me now see if he is harboured . ''
Parsing [sent. 691 len. 35]: They walked round the thicket , and could not find any slot or track by which the stag had left the covert , and Jacob pronounced that the animal must be hid in it .
Parsing [sent. 692 len. 49]: `` Now , Edward , do you stay here while I go back to the lee side of the covert : I will enter it with Smoker , and the stag will , in all probability , when he is roused , come out to breast the wind .
Parsing [sent. 693 len. 82]: You will then have a good shot at him ; recollect to fire so as to hit him behind the shoulder : if he is moving quick , fire a little before the shoulders ; if slow , take aim accurately ; but recollect , if I come upon him in the covert , I shall kill him if I can , for we want the venison , and then we will go after another to give you a chance . ''
Parsing [sent. 694 len. 23]: Jacob then left Edward , and went down to the lee side of the covert , where he entered it with Smoker .

Parsing [sent. 697 len. 29]: Remembering the advice of Jacob , Edward remained where he was , in silence reloading his piece , and was soon afterwards joined by Jacob and the dog .
Parsing [sent. 698 len. 7]: `` Well done , Edward ! ''

Parsing [sent. 752 len. 10]: This was now a busy time at the cottage .

Parsing [sent. 757 len. 48]: Little Edith had also employment now ; for the hens began to lay eggs , and as soon as she heard them cackling , she ran for the eggs and brought them in ; and before the month was over Jacob had set four hens upon eggs .

Parsing [sent. 772 len. 16]: Alice , if we only had a cow , would n't that be jolly ? ''

Parsing [sent. 1670 len. 15]: Hold to the farm as it now is ; it will support you all .
Parsing [sent. 1671 len. 23]: My dear Alice and Edith , I am dying ; very soon I shall be laid by your brothers in my grave .
Parsing [sent. 1672 len. 13]: Be good children , and look up to your brothers for everything .
Parsing [sent. 1673 len. 33]: And now , kiss me , Alice : you have been a great comfort to me , for you have read the Bible to me when I could no longer read myself .
Parsing [sent. 1674 len. 26]: May your deathbed be as well attended as mine has been , and may you live happily , and die the death of a Christian !
Parsing [sent. 1675 len. 8]: Good-bye , and may God bless you .
Parsing [sent. 1676 len. 19]: Bless you , Edith ; may you grow up as good and as innocent as you are now .
Parsing [sent. 1677 len. 19]: Farewell , Humphrey -- farewell , Edward -- my eyes are dim -- pray for me , children .
Parsing [sent. 1678 len. 19]: O God of mercy -- pardon my many sins , and receive my soul , through Jesus Christ .
Parsing [sent. 1679 len. 5]: Amen , amen . ''
Parsing [sent. 1680 len. 11]: These were the last words spoken by the old forester .
Parsing [sent. 1681 len. 30]: The children , who were kneeling by the side of the bed , praying as he had requested , when they rose up , found that he was dead .
Parsing [sent. 1682 len. 14]: They all wept bitterly , for they dearly loved the good old man .
Parsing [sent. 1683 len. 25]: Alice remained sobbing in Edward 's arms , and Edith in Humphrey 's , and it was long before the brothers could console them .
Parsing [sent. 1684 len. 24]: Humphrey at last said to Alice , `` You hurt poor Edward 's arm -- you do n't know how painful it is !
Parsing [sent. 1685 len. 22]: Come , dears , let us go into the other room , and get something to take the pain away . ''
Parsing [sent. 1686 len. 26]: These requests diverted the attention at the same time that it roused fresh sympathy in the little girls -- they all went into the sitting-room .
Parsing [sent. 1687 len. 29]: Humphrey gave his sisters some potatoes to scrape upon a piece of linen , while he took off Edward 's coat , and turned up his shirt sleeves .
Parsing [sent. 1688 len. 19]: The scraped potatoes were then laid on the burn , and Edward said they gave him great relief .
Parsing [sent. 1689 len. 21]: Some more were then scraped by the little girls , who could not , however , repress their occasional sobs .
Parsing [sent. 1690 len. 21]: Humphrey then told them that Edward had had nothing to eat , and that they must get him some supper .
Parsing [sent. 1691 len. 22]: This again occupied them for some time ; and when the supper was ready , they all sat down to it .
Parsing [sent. 1692 len. 40]: They went to bed early , but not before Edward had read a chapter out of the Bible , and the prayers , as old Jacob had always done ; and this again caused their tears to flow afresh .
Parsing [sent. 1693 len. 18]: `` Come , Alice dear , you and Edith must go to bed , '' said Humphrey .
Parsing [sent. 1694 len. 36]: The little girls threw themselves into their brothers ' arms ; and having wept for some time , Alice raised herself , and taking Edith by the hand , led her away to the bedroom .
Parsing [sent. 1695 len. 3]: CHAPTER TEN .
Parsing [sent. 1696 len. 17]: `` Humphrey , '' said Edward , `` the sooner all this is over the better .
Parsing [sent. 1697 len. 23]: As long as poor Jacob 's body remains in the cottage there will be nothing but distress with the poor girls . ''
Parsing [sent. 1698 len. 18]: `` I agree with you , '' replied Humphrey ; `` where shall we bury him ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1699 len. 17]: `` Under the great oak-tree , at the back of the cottage , '' replied Edward .
Parsing [sent. 1700 len. 26]: `` One day the old man said to me that he should like to be buried under one of the oaks of the forest . ''
Parsing [sent. 1701 len. 33]: `` Well then , I will go and dig his grave to-night , '' replied Humphrey ; `` the moon is bright , and I shall have it finished before morning . ''
Parsing [sent. 1702 len. 14]: `` I am sorry that I can not help you , Humphrey . ''
Parsing [sent. 1703 len. 17]: `` I am sorry that you are hurt ; but I want no help , Edward .
Parsing [sent. 1704 len. 16]: If you will lie down a little , perhaps you will be able to sleep .
Parsing [sent. 1705 len. 12]: Let us change the potato poultice before you go on . ''
Parsing [sent. 1706 len. 28]: Humphrey put the fresh dressing on Edward 's arm ; and Edward , who was very much exhausted , lay down in his clothes on the bed .
Parsing [sent. 1707 len. 25]: Humphrey went out , and having found his tools , set to his task ; he worked hard , and before morning had finished .
Parsing [sent. 1708 len. 25]: He then went in , and took his place on the bed by the side of Edward , who was in a sound sleep .
Parsing [sent. 1709 len. 9]: At daylight Humphrey rose , and waked Edward .
Parsing [sent. 1710 len. 29]: `` All is ready , Edward ; but I fear you must help me to put poor Jacob in the cart ; do you think you can ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1711 len. 22]: `` Oh yes ; my arm is much easier , and I feel very different from what I did last night .
Parsing [sent. 1712 len. 20]: If you will go and get the cart I will see what I can do in the meantime . ''
Parsing [sent. 1713 len. 28]: When Humphrey returned he found Edward had selected a sheet to wind the body in , but could not do more till Humphrey came to help him .
Parsing [sent. 1714 len. 23]: They then wrapped it round the body , and carried it out of the cottage , and put it into the cart .
Parsing [sent. 1715 len. 12]: `` Now , Edward , shall we call our sisters ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1716 len. 25]: `` No , not yet ; let us have the body laid in the grave first , and then we will call them . ''
Parsing [sent. 1717 len. 30]: They dragged the body on the cart to the grave , and laid it in it , and then returned back and put the pony in the stable again .
Parsing [sent. 1718 len. 13]: `` Are there not prayers proper for reading over the dead ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1719 len. 3]: said Humphrey .
Parsing [sent. 1720 len. 29]: `` I believe that there are , but they are not in the Bible ; so we must read some portion of the Bible , '' said Edward .
Parsing [sent. 1721 len. 58]: `` Yes , I think there is one of the Psalms which it would be right to read , Edward , '' said Humphrey , turning over the leaves ; `` here it is , the ninetieth , in which you recollect it says ` that the days of man are threescore years and ten . ' ''
Parsing [sent. 1722 len. 21]: `` Yes , '' replied Edward , `` and we will read this one also , -- the 146th . ''
Parsing [sent. 1723 len. 11]: `` Are our sisters risen , do you think ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1724 len. 21]: `` I am sure that they are , '' replied Humphrey , `` and I will go to them . ''
Parsing [sent. 1725 len. 21]: Humphrey went to the door , and said , `` Alice -- Alice and Edith -- come out immediately . ''
Parsing [sent. 1726 len. 6]: They were both ready dressed .
Parsing [sent. 1727 len. 14]: Edward took the Bible under his arm , and Alice by the hand .
Parsing [sent. 1728 len. 25]: Humphrey led Edith until they arrived at the grave , when the two little girls saw the covered body of Jacob lying in it .
Parsing [sent. 1729 len. 12]: `` Kneel down , '' said Edward , opening the Bible .
Parsing [sent. 1730 len. 9]: And they all knelt down by the grave .
Parsing [sent. 1731 len. 12]: Edward read the two Psalms , and then closed the book .
Parsing [sent. 1732 len. 20]: The little girls took one last look at the body , and then turned away weeping to the cottage .
Parsing [sent. 1733 len. 15]: Edward and Humphrey filled up the grave , and then followed their sisters home .
Parsing [sent. 1734 len. 16]: `` I 'm glad it 's over , '' said Humphrey , wiping his eyes .
Parsing [sent. 1735 len. 5]: `` Poor old Jacob !
Parsing [sent. 1736 len. 10]: I 'll put a paling round his grave . ''
Parsing [sent. 1737 len. 10]: `` Come in , Humphrey , '' said Edward .
Parsing [sent. 1738 len. 17]: Edward sat down upon old Jacob 's chair , and took Alice and Edith to him .
Parsing [sent. 1739 len. 38]: Putting his arm round each , he said : `` Alice and Edith , my dear little sisters , we have lost a good friend , and one to whose memory we can not be too grateful .
Parsing [sent. 1740 len. 24]: He saved us from perishing in the flames which burnt down our father 's house , and has protected us here ever since .
Parsing [sent. 1741 len. 48]: He is gone ; for it has pleased God to summon him to Him , and we must bow to the will of Heaven ; and here we are , brothers and sisters , orphans , and with no one to look to for protection but Heaven .
Parsing [sent. 1742 len. 17]: Here we are , away from the rest of the world , living for one another .
Parsing [sent. 1743 len. 6]: What then must we do ?
Parsing [sent. 1744 len. 12]: We must love one another dearly , and help one another .
Parsing [sent. 1745 len. 26]: I will do my part , if my life is spared , and so will Humphrey , and so will you , my dear sisters .
Parsing [sent. 1746 len. 6]: I can answer for all .
Parsing [sent. 1747 len. 49]: Now it is no use to lament -- we must all work , and work cheerfully ; and we will pray every morning and every night that God will bless our endeavours , and enable us to provide for ourselves , and live here in peace and safety .
Parsing [sent. 1748 len. 17]: Kiss me , dear Alice and Edith , and kiss Humphrey , and kiss one another .
Parsing [sent. 1749 len. 30]: Let these kisses be the seals to our bond ; and let us put our trust in Him who only is a father to the widow and the orphan .
Parsing [sent. 1750 len. 7]: And now let us pray . ''
Parsing [sent. 1751 len. 15]: Edward and the children repeated the Lord 's Prayer , and then rose up .
Parsing [sent. 1752 len. 39]: They went to their respective employments , and the labour of the day soon made them composed , although then , for many days afterwards , it was but occasionally that a smile was seen upon their lips .
Parsing [sent. 1753 len. 36]: Thus passed a week , by which time Edward 's arm was so far well that it gave him no pain , and he was able to assist Humphrey in the work on the farm .
Parsing [sent. 1754 len. 23]: The snow had disappeared , and the spring , although it had been checked for a time , now made rapid advances .
Parsing [sent. 1755 len. 59]: Constant occupation and the return of fine weather both had the effect of restoring the serenity of their minds ; and while Humphrey was preparing the paling to fix round the grave of old Jacob , Alice and Edith collected the wild violets which now peeped forth on sheltered spots , and planted the roots over the grave .
Parsing [sent. 1756 len. 61]: Edward also procured all the early flowers he could collect , and assisted his sisters in their task ; and thus , in planting it , and putting up the paling , the grave of the old man became their constant work-ground ; and when their labour was done , they would still remain there and talk over his worth .
Parsing [sent. 1757 len. 84]: The Sunday following the burial , the weather being fine and warm , Edward proposed that they should read the usual service , which had been selected by old Jacob , at the grave , and not in the cottage , as formerly ; and this they continued afterwards to do , whenever the weather would permit ; thus did old Jacob 's resting-placing become their church , and overpower them with those feelings of love and devotion which give efficacy to prayer .
Parsing [sent. 1758 len. 29]: As soon as the paling was finished Humphrey put up a board against the oak-tree , with the simple words carved on it , `` Jacob Armitage . ''
Parsing [sent. 1759 len. 40]: Edward had every day expected that Oswald Partridge would have called upon him , as he had promised to do before the week was out ; but Oswald had not made his appearance , much to Edward 's surprise .
Parsing [sent. 1760 len. 19]: A month passed away ; Edward 's arm was now quite well , and still Oswald came not .
Parsing [sent. 1761 len. 55]: One morning Humphrey and Edward were conversing upon many points -- the principal of which was upon Edward going to Lymington , for they were now in want of flour and meal -- when Edward thought of what old Jacob had told him relative to the money that he would find in his chest .
Parsing [sent. 1762 len. 60]: He went into Jacob 's room and opened the chest , at the bottom of which , under the clothes , he found a leather bag , which he brought out to Humphrey ; on opening it , they were much surprised to find in it more than sixty gold pieces , besides a great deal of silver coin .
Parsing [sent. 1763 len. 14]: `` Surely this is a great sum of money , '' observed Humphrey .
Parsing [sent. 1764 len. 27]: `` I do n't know what is the price of things but it appears to me that it ought to last us a long while . ''
Parsing [sent. 1765 len. 10]: `` I think so too , '' replied Edward .
Parsing [sent. 1766 len. 17]: `` I wish Oswald Partridge would come , for I want to ask him many questions .
Parsing [sent. 1767 len. 28]: I do n't know the price of flour or anything else we have to purchase , nor do I know what ought to be paid for venison .
Parsing [sent. 1768 len. 17]: I do n't like to go to Lymington till I see him , for that reason .
Parsing [sent. 1769 len. 18]: If he does not come soon I shall ride over and see what is the matter . ''
Parsing [sent. 1770 len. 26]: Edward then replaced the money in the chest , and he and Humphrey then went out to the farm-yard to go on with their work .
Parsing [sent. 1771 len. 19]: It was not until six weeks after the death of old Jacob that Oswald Partridge made his appearance .
Parsing [sent. 1772 len. 10]: `` How is the old man , sir ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1773 len. 5]: was his first question .
Parsing [sent. 1774 len. 15]: `` He was buried a few days after you left , '' replied Edward .
Parsing [sent. 1775 len. 11]: `` I expected as much , '' said the forester .
Parsing [sent. 1776 len. 12]: `` Peace be with him -- he was a good man .
Parsing [sent. 1777 len. 7]: And how is your arm ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1778 len. 8]: `` Nearly well , '' replied Edward .
Parsing [sent. 1779 len. 38]: `` Now , sit down , Oswald , for I have a great deal to say to you ; and first let me ask you what has detained you from coming here according to your promise ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1780 len. 11]: `` Simply , and in few words -- murder . ''
Parsing [sent. 1781 len. 4]: `` Murder ! ''
Parsing [sent. 1782 len. 3]: exclaimed Edward .
Parsing [sent. 1783 len. 25]: `` Yes , deliberate murder , sir ; in short , they have beheaded the king -- beheaded King Charles , our sovereign . ''
Parsing [sent. 1784 len. 9]: `` Have they dared to do it ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1785 len. 8]: `` They have , '' replied Oswald .
Parsing [sent. 1786 len. 34]: `` We know little that is going on in the forest ; but when I saw you last I heard that he was then in London , and was to be tried . ''
Parsing [sent. 1787 len. 4]: `` Tried ! ''
Parsing [sent. 1788 len. 3]: exclaimed Edward .
Parsing [sent. 1789 len. 8]: `` How could they try a king ?
Parsing [sent. 1790 len. 22]: By the laws of our country a man must be tried by his equals ; and where were his equals ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1791 len. 21]: `` Majesty becomes nought , I suppose , '' replied Oswald ; `` but still it is as I say .
Parsing [sent. 1792 len. 43]: Two days after you left the Intendant hastened up to London ; and from what I have understood , he was strongly opposed to the deed , and did all he could to prevent it , but it was of no use .
Parsing [sent. 1793 len. 57]: When he left he gave me strict injunctions not to go away from the cottage for an hour , as his daughter was left alone , and as I promised , I could not come to you ; but , nevertheless , Patience received letters from him , and told me what I tell you . ''
Parsing [sent. 1794 len. 9]: `` You have not dined , Oswald ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1795 len. 3]: said Edward .
Parsing [sent. 1796 len. 9]: `` No , that I have not . ''

Parsing [sent. 1799 len. 30]: Your intelligence has so astounded me that I can listen to nothing else till I have had a little while to commune with myself and subdue my feelings . ''
Parsing [sent. 1800 len. 13]: Edward was indeed in a state of mind which required calming down .
Parsing [sent. 1801 len. 17]: He quitted the cottage and walked out for some distance into the forest in deep thought .
Parsing [sent. 1802 len. 6]: `` Murdered at last ! ''
Parsing [sent. 1803 len. 3]: exclaimed he .
Parsing [sent. 1804 len. 30]: `` Yes , well may it be called murder , and no one to save him -- not a blow struck in his defence -- not an arm raised .
Parsing [sent. 1805 len. 10]: How much gallant blood has been shed in vain !
Parsing [sent. 1806 len. 18]: Spirit of my fathers -- didst thou leave none of thy mettle and thy honour behind thee ?
Parsing [sent. 1807 len. 7]: Or has all England become craven ?
Parsing [sent. 1808 len. 34]: Well , the time will come ; and if I can no longer hope to fight for my king , at all events I can fight against those who have murdered him . ''
Parsing [sent. 1809 len. 58]: Such were Edward 's thoughts as he wandered through the forest , and more than an hour elapsed before his impetuous blood could return to its usual flow ; at last , more calm , he returned to the cottage , and listened to the details which Oswald now gave to him of what he had heard .
Parsing [sent. 1810 len. 14]: When Oswald had finished , Edward asked him whether the Intendant had returned .
Parsing [sent. 1811 len. 15]: `` Yes , or I should not have been here , '' replied Oswald .
Parsing [sent. 1812 len. 25]: `` He came back yesterday , looking most disconsolate and grave , and I hear that he returns to London in a few days .
Parsing [sent. 1813 len. 20]: Indeed , he told me so himself , for I requested permission to come over to see your grandfather .
Parsing [sent. 1814 len. 20]: He said that I might go , but must return soon , as he must go back to London .
Parsing [sent. 1815 len. 64]: I believe , from what Miss Patience told me , and what I have seen myself , that he is sincerely amazed and vexed at what has taken place ; and so indeed are many more , who , although opposed to the king 's method of government , never had an idea that things should have turned out as they have done .
Parsing [sent. 1816 len. 35]: I have a message from him to you , which is , that he begs you will come to see him , that he may thank you for the preservation of his child . ''
Parsing [sent. 1817 len. 26]: `` I will take his thanks from you , Oswald : that will do as well as if he gave them me in person . ''
Parsing [sent. 1818 len. 23]: `` Yes , perhaps so ; but I have another message from another party , which is , the young lady herself .
Parsing [sent. 1819 len. 53]: She desires me to tell you that she will never be happy till she has seen you , and thanked you for your courage and kindness ; and that you have no right to put her under such an obligation , and not give her an opportunity of expressing what she feels .
Parsing [sent. 1820 len. 30]: Now , Mr Edward , I am certain that she is earnest in what she says , and she made me promise that I would persuade you to come .
Parsing [sent. 1821 len. 50]: I could not refuse her , for she is a dear little creature ; as her father will go to London in a few days , you may ride over and see her without any fear of being affronted by any offers which he may make to you . ''
Parsing [sent. 1822 len. 42]: `` Well , '' replied Edward , `` I have no great objection to see her again , for she was very kind to me ; and as you say that the Intendant will not be there I perhaps may come .
Parsing [sent. 1823 len. 12]: But now I must talk to you about other matters . ''
Parsing [sent. 1824 len. 26]: Edward then put many questions to Oswald relative to the value of various articles , and to the best method of disposing of his venison .
Parsing [sent. 1825 len. 16]: Oswald answered all his questions , and Edward took down notes and directions on paper .
Parsing [sent. 1826 len. 34]: Oswald remained with them for two days , and then bade them farewell , exacting a promise from Edward that he would come to the ranger 's cottage as soon as he could .
Parsing [sent. 1827 len. 43]: `` Should the Intendant come back before he is expected , I will come over and let you know ; but I think , from what I heard him say , he expected to be at least a month in London . ''
Parsing [sent. 1828 len. 21]: Edward promised that Oswald should see him in less than ten days , and Oswald set out on his journey .
Parsing [sent. 1829 len. 27]: `` Humphrey , '' said Edward , as soon as Oswald was gone , `` I have made up my mind to go to Lymington to-morrow .

Parsing [sent. 1832 len. 3]: replied Humphrey .

Parsing [sent. 1835 len. 19]: I do not consider there is any danger , I admit ; but something might happen to them .
Parsing [sent. 1836 len. 6]: I should never forgive myself .
Parsing [sent. 1837 len. 31]: Still , it is necessary that you should go to Lymington with me some time or another , that you may know where to purchase and sell , if required .

Parsing [sent. 1839 len. 18]: We will then leave him in charge of our sisters , and go to Lymington together . ''
Parsing [sent. 1840 len. 15]: `` You are right , Edward ; that will be the best plan . ''
Parsing [sent. 1841 len. 11]: As Humphrey made this remark , Oswald re-entered the cottage .

Parsing [sent. 1843 len. 13]: `` It is of no consequence whether I return now or to-morrow .
Parsing [sent. 1844 len. 25]: It is now early , and as you intend going to Lymington , it occurred to me that I had better go with you .
Parsing [sent. 1845 len. 20]: I can then show you all you want , which will be much better than going by yourself . ''

Parsing [sent. 1848 len. 7]: Will you get it , Humphrey ?
Parsing [sent. 1849 len. 14]: For I must go for some money , and speak to Alice . ''
Parsing [sent. 1850 len. 61]: Humphrey went immediately to put the pony in the cart , when Edward said : `` Oswald , you must not call me Mr Edward , even when we are alone ; if you do , you will be calling me so before other people , and , therefore , recollect in future , it must be plain Edward . ''
Parsing [sent. 1851 len. 33]: `` Since you wish it , certainly , '' replied Oswald ; `` indeed it would be better ; for a slip of the tongue before other people might create suspicion . ''
Parsing [sent. 1852 len. 29]: The pony and cart were soon at the door , and Edward , having received further instructions from Alice , set off for Lymington , accompanied by Oswald .
Parsing [sent. 1853 len. 3]: CHAPTER ELEVEN .
Parsing [sent. 1854 len. 11]: `` Could you have found your way to Lymington ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1855 len. 9]: said Oswald , as the pony trotted along .
Parsing [sent. 1856 len. 21]: `` Yes , I think so , '' replied Edward ; `` but I must have first gone to Arnwood .
Parsing [sent. 1857 len. 23]: Indeed , had I been alone , I should have done so ; but we have made a much shorter cut . ''
Parsing [sent. 1858 len. 22]: `` I did not think that you would have liked to have seen the ruins of Arnwood , '' replied Oswald .
Parsing [sent. 1859 len. 15]: `` Not a day passes without my thinking of them , '' replied Edward .
Parsing [sent. 1860 len. 8]: `` I should like to see them .
Parsing [sent. 1861 len. 23]: I should like to see if any one has taken possession of the property ; for they say it is confiscated . ''

Parsing [sent. 1863 len. 11]: I have not seen it for more than a year .
Parsing [sent. 1864 len. 11]: I hardly think that any one will recognise you . ''
Parsing [sent. 1865 len. 14]: `` I should think not ; but I care little if they do .
Parsing [sent. 1866 len. 10]: Indeed , who is there to know me ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1867 len. 30]: `` Well , my introduction of you will save some surmises , probably ; and I shall not take you among those who may be inclined to ask questions .
Parsing [sent. 1868 len. 21]: See , there is the steeple ; we have not more than a quarter of an hour 's drive . ''
Parsing [sent. 1869 len. 26]: As soon as they arrived at Lymington , Oswald directed the way to a small hostelrie , to which the keepers and verderers usually resorted .
Parsing [sent. 1870 len. 22]: In fact , the landlord was the party who took all the venison off their hands , and disposed of it .
Parsing [sent. 1871 len. 41]: They drove into the yard , and , giving the pony and cart in charge of the hostler , went into the inn , where they found the landlord , and one or two other people , who were drinking .
Parsing [sent. 1872 len. 11]: `` Well , Master Andrew , how fare you ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1873 len. 3]: said Oswald .
Parsing [sent. 1874 len. 41]: `` Let me see , '' said the corpulent landlord , throwing back his head , and putting out his stomach , as he peered at Oswald ; `` why , Oswald Partridge , as I am a born man .
Parsing [sent. 1875 len. 10]: Where have you been this many a day ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1876 len. 18]: `` In the forest , Master Andrew , where there are no few chops and changes . ''
Parsing [sent. 1877 len. 25]: `` Yes , I heard you have a sort of Parliamentary keeper , I 'm told ; and who is this with you ? ''

Parsing [sent. 1879 len. 7]: `` Jacob dead , poor fellow !
Parsing [sent. 1880 len. 15]: As true as flint was Jacob Armitage , as I 'm a born man !
Parsing [sent. 1881 len. 6]: And so he is dead !
Parsing [sent. 1882 len. 9]: Well , we all owe heaven a death .
Parsing [sent. 1883 len. 14]: Foresters and landlords , as well as kings , all must die ! ''
Parsing [sent. 1884 len. 17]: `` I have brought Edward Armitage over here to introduce him to you , Master Andrew .
Parsing [sent. 1885 len. 18]: Now that the old man is dead , you must look to him for forest meat . ''
Parsing [sent. 1886 len. 12]: `` Oh , well , well , it is scarce now .
Parsing [sent. 1887 len. 9]: I have not had any for some time .
Parsing [sent. 1888 len. 7]: Old Jacob brought me the last .
Parsing [sent. 1889 len. 15]: You are not one of the Parliamentary foresters , then , I presume ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1890 len. 8]: continued the landlord , turning to Edward .
Parsing [sent. 1891 len. 16]: `` No , '' replied Edward , `` I kill no venison for Roundheads . ''
Parsing [sent. 1892 len. 11]: `` Right , my sapling ; right and well said .
Parsing [sent. 1893 len. 26]: The Armitages were all good men and true , and followed the fortunes of the Beverleys ; but there are no Beverleys to follow now .
Parsing [sent. 1894 len. 11]: Cut off root and branch -- more 's the pity .
Parsing [sent. 1895 len. 6]: That was a sad business .
Parsing [sent. 1896 len. 31]: But come in ; we must not talk here , for walls have ears , they say , and one never knows who one dares to speak before now . ''
Parsing [sent. 1897 len. 81]: Oswald and Edward then entered with the landlord , and arrangements were made between Master Andrew and the latter for a regular supply of venison during the season at a certain price ; but as it would now be dangerous to bring it into the town , it was agreed that when there was any ready , Edward should come to Lymington and give notice , and the landlord would send out people to bring it in during the night .
Parsing [sent. 1898 len. 24]: This bargain concluded , they took a glass with the landlord , and then went into the town to make the necessary purchases .

Parsing [sent. 1908 len. 63]: Edward remained silent for some little while , for he could not trust himself to speak ; at last he replied : `` To be candid with you , I am , and all my family have been , followers of the Beverley family , and I should be sorry if the colonel 's sword was to fall into any other hands .
