Parsing [sent. 2097 len. 29]: Perceiving the danger that his brother was in , and that , moreover , Humphrey himself was not aware of it , he hardly knew how to act .
Parsing [sent. 2098 len. 44]: The bull was too far from him to fire at it with any chance of success ; and how to let Humphrey know that the animal had discovered him and was making towards him , without calling out , he did not know .
Parsing [sent. 2099 len. 44]: All this was the thought of a moment , and then Edward determined to fire at the bull nearest to him , which he had promised not to do till Humphrey was also ready to fire ; and after firing to call Humphrey .
Parsing [sent. 2100 len. 72]: He , therefore , for one moment , turned away from his brother , and , taking aim at the bull , fired his gun ; but probably from his nerves being a little shaken at the idea of Humphrey being in danger , the wound was not mortal , and the bull galloped back to the herd , which formed a closed phalanx about a quarter of a mile distant .
Parsing [sent. 2101 len. 54]: Edward then turned to where his brother was , and perceived that the bull had not made off with the rest of the cattle , but was within thirty yards of Humphrey , and advancing upon him , and that Humphrey was standing up beside the tree with his gun ready to fire .
Parsing [sent. 2102 len. 57]: Humphrey fired , and , as it appeared , he also missed his aim ; the animal made at him ; but Humphrey , with great quickness , dropped his gun , and , swinging by the lower boughs , was into the tree , and out of the bull 's reach , in a moment .
Parsing [sent. 2103 len. 33]: Edward smiled when he perceived that Humphrey was safe ; but still he was a prisoner , for the bull went round and round the tree roaring and looking up at Humphrey .
Parsing [sent. 2104 len. 19]: Edward thought a minute , then loaded his gun and ordered Smoker to run in to the bull .
Parsing [sent. 2105 len. 24]: The dog , who had only been restrained by Edward 's keeping him down at his feet , sprang forward to the attack .
Parsing [sent. 2106 len. 54]: Edward had intended , by calling to the dog , to induce the bull to follow it till within gunshot ; but before the bull had been attacked , Edward observed that one or two more of the bulls had left the herd , and were coming at a rapid pace towards him .
Parsing [sent. 2107 len. 34]: Under these circumstances , Edward perceived that his only chance was to climb into a tree himself , which he did , taking good care to take his gun and ammunition with him .
Parsing [sent. 2108 len. 18]: Having safely fixed himself in a forked bough , Edward then surveyed the position of the parties .
Parsing [sent. 2109 len. 11]: There was Humphrey in the tree , without his gun .
Parsing [sent. 2110 len. 34]: The bull who had pursued Humphrey was now running at Smoker , who appeared to be aware that he was to decoy the bull towards Edward , for he kept retreating towards him .
Parsing [sent. 2111 len. 41]: In the meantime the two other bulls were quite close at hand , mingling their bellowing and roaring with the first ; and one of them as near to Edward as the first bull , which was engaged with Smoker .
Parsing [sent. 2112 len. 31]: At last one of the advancing bulls stood still , pawing the ground as if disappointed at not finding an enemy , not forty yards from where Edward was perched .
Parsing [sent. 2113 len. 14]: Edward took good aim , and when he fired the bull fell dead .
Parsing [sent. 2114 len. 59]: Edward was reloading his piece when he heard a howl , and looking round saw Smoker flying up in the air , having been tossed by the first bull ; and at the same time he observed that Humphrey had descended from the tree , recovered his gun , and was now safe again upon the lower bough .
Parsing [sent. 2115 len. 77]: The first bull was advancing again to attack Smoker , who appeared incapable of getting away , so much was he injured by the fall , when the other bull , who apparently must have been an old antagonist of the first , roared and attacked him ; and now the two boys were up in the tree , the two bulls fighting between them , and Smoker lying on the ground , panting and exhausted .
Parsing [sent. 2116 len. 24]: As the bulls , with locked horns , were furiously pressing each other , both guns were discharged , and both animals fell .
Parsing [sent. 2117 len. 34]: After waiting a little while to see if they rose again , or if any more of the herd came up , Edward and Humphrey descended from the trees and heartily shook hands .

Parsing [sent. 2196 len. 22]: Holdfast , as well as Humphrey 's puppy , which had been named Watch , had grown very fine young animals .
Parsing [sent. 2197 len. 88]: The first had been named Holdfast , because it would seize the pigs by the ears and lead them into the sty , and the other because it was so alert at the least noise : but , as Humphrey said , Watch ought to have learnt to lead the pigs , it being more in his line of business than Holdfast 's , which was to be brought up for hunting in the forest , while Watch was being educated as a house and farm-yard dog .

Parsing [sent. 2798 len. 3]: CHAPTER FIFTEEN .

Parsing [sent. 3114 len. 7]: `` And your Christian name ? ''
Parsing [sent. 3115 len. 15]: `` I will tell you by and by , '' replied he , confused .
Parsing [sent. 3116 len. 61]: They arrived at the cottage , and Edward and Pablo were busy unpacking the cart , and putting all the contents into the inner chamber , where Pablo now slept , when Alice , who , with Edith , had been talking to the boy , came to Edward and said -- `` Edward , she 's a girl ! ''

Parsing [sent. 3122 len. 23]: `` Well , then , '' replied Edward , `` you must make up a bed for her in your room to-night .

Parsing [sent. 3126 len. 5]: said Alice , laughing .
Parsing [sent. 3127 len. 36]: `` Yes ; she will make a nice little wife for him some years hence ; and she may prove an heiress perhaps , for there is an iron chest with money in it . ''
Parsing [sent. 3128 len. 17]: Alice returned to her new companion , and Edward and Pablo continued to unload the cart .

Parsing [sent. 3130 len. 22]: `` Oh yes , '' replied Pablo , `` very handsome girl ; but too much girl for handsome boy . ''
Parsing [sent. 3131 len. 44]: At last everything was out of the cart , the iron chest dragged into Pablo 's room , and Billy put into his stable and given his supper , which he had well earned , for the cart had been very heavily loaded .
Parsing [sent. 3132 len. 31]: They then all sat down to supper , Edward saying to their new acquaintance -- `` So I find that I am to have another sister instead of another brother .
Parsing [sent. 3133 len. 9]: Now you will tell me your name ? ''

Parsing [sent. 3135 len. 15]: `` And why did you not tell me that you are a girl ? ''

Parsing [sent. 3137 len. 6]: My poor dear father ! ''

Parsing [sent. 3139 len. 15]: Alice and Edith kissed her and consoled her , and she became calm again .

Parsing [sent. 3141 len. 16]: `` We have much to be thankful for , my dears , '' said Edward .
Parsing [sent. 3142 len. 46]: `` I am sure I feel that I have been in great danger , and I only wish that I had been more useful than I have been ; but it has been the will of God , and we must not arraign His decrees .
Parsing [sent. 3143 len. 35]: Let us return thanks for His great mercies , and bow in submission to His dispensations , and pray that He will give peace to poor little Clara , and soften her affliction . ''

Parsing [sent. 3145 len. 24]: When they had finished , Alice led her away to her bedroom , followed by Edith , and they put her to bed .
Parsing [sent. 3146 len. 18]: Edward and Pablo also retired , both worn out by the fatigue and excitement of the day .
Parsing [sent. 3147 len. 26]: They were up on the following morning at day-dawn , and , putting Billy in the cart , set off for the cottage of Clara .
Parsing [sent. 3148 len. 119]: They found everything as they had left it , and , having loaded the cart with what had been left behind the day before , the bedding for two beds , with several articles of furniture which Edward thought might be useful , there being still a little room left , Edward packed up in a wooden case with dried fern all the wine that was in the cupboard ; and , having assisted Pablo in forcing the cart once more through the path in the wood , he left him to return home with the cart , while he remained to wait the arrival of Humphrey , and whoever might come with him from the Intendant 's .
Parsing [sent. 3149 len. 37]: About ten o'clock , as he was watching outside of the wood , he perceived several people approaching him , and soon made out that Humphrey , the Intendant , and Oswald were among the number .

Parsing [sent. 3151 len. 14]: The Intendant was on horseback , but all the rest were on foot .

Parsing [sent. 3153 len. 51]: He appeared to be very grave and thoughtful , and Edward thought that there was a coolness in his manner towards himself , -- for it must be recollected that Mr Heatherstone had not seen Edward since he had rendered him such service in saving the life of his daughter .
Parsing [sent. 3154 len. 33]: The consequence was , that Edward felt somewhat indignant , but he did not express his feelings , by his looks even , but conveyed the party in silence to the cottage .

Parsing [sent. 3164 len. 43]: The Intendant called his clerk , who had accompanied him , and desired him to get ready his writing materials , and then said -- `` Edward Armitage , we will now take down your deposition as to what has occurred . ''
Parsing [sent. 3165 len. 39]: Edward then commenced by stating `` that he was out in the forest and had lost his way , and was seeking his way home -- '' `` You were out in the forest during the night ? ''

Parsing [sent. 3178 len. 10]: Edward met with no more interruption in his narrative .

Parsing [sent. 3181 len. 18]: `` I should hope so , '' replied Edward , taking the pen and signing his name .

Parsing [sent. 3184 len. 16]: `` May I ask if my presence is considered any longer to be necessary ? ''

Parsing [sent. 3219 len. 81]: Perceiving the entrance of Edward , he again took his seat at the table , and after a pause said -- `` Edward Armitage , that you have been brought up very superior to your station in life is certain ; and that you are loyal , bold , and resolute , is equally so ; you have put me under an obligation which I never can repay , even if you allowed me to exert myself in your behalf .

Parsing [sent. 3227 len. 59]: If any other man in the county , who is of your opinion , was to express himself , now that your cause is hopeless , as you have done , the prisons would be crowded , the executions would be daily , and the cause would be in proportion weakened by the loss of the most daring .
Parsing [sent. 3228 len. 17]: ` Bide your time ' is a good motto , and I recommend it to you .

Parsing [sent. 3241 len. 52]: So much so , indeed , as to have incurred not only the suspicion but the ill-will of Cromwell , who , I fear , is now making rapid advances towards that absolute authority for which the king has suffered , and which he would now vest in his own person .
Parsing [sent. 3242 len. 79]: I considered that our cause was just ; and , had the power been left in the hands of those who would have exercised it with discretion and moderation , the king would even now have been on the throne , and the liberties of his subjects sacred ; but it is easier to put a vast and powerful engine into motion than to stop it ; and such has been the case in this unfortunate civil war .

Parsing [sent. 3298 len. 55]: `` Come here , Clara , '' said the Intendant gently ; `` you do not know perhaps that I am your sincere friend ; and now that your father is dead , I want you to come and live with my daughter , who will be delighted to have you as a companion .

Parsing [sent. 3300 len. 15]: And I will take care of you and be a father to you . ''

Parsing [sent. 3973 len. 3]: CHAPTER TWENTY .
Parsing [sent. 3974 len. 24]: The summer had now advanced , when Oswald one day said to Edward -- `` Have you heard the news , sir ? ''

Parsing [sent. 3978 len. 9]: replied Edward , `` that is news indeed !
Parsing [sent. 3979 len. 10]: The Intendant has never mentioned it to me . ''
Parsing [sent. 3980 len. 23]: `` I daresay not , sir ; for he knows your feelings , and would be sorry to part with you . ''

Parsing [sent. 3993 len. 18]: `` Perhaps you may be persuaded to the contrary before we part , '' replied the Intendant .

Parsing [sent. 4002 len. 114]: The Intendant handed three letters to Edward , by which it was evident that all the king 's friends in England were of opinion that the time was not ripe for the attempt , and that it would be only a sacrifice to stir in the matter ; that the Scotch army raised was composed of those who were the greatest enemies to the king , and that the best thing that could happen for the king 's interest would be that they were destroyed by Cromwell ; that it was impossible for the English adherents of Charles to join them , and that the Scotch did not wish them so to do .
Parsing [sent. 4003 len. 24]: `` You are no politician , Edward , '' said the Intendant smiling , as Edward laid the letters down on the table .

Parsing [sent. 4197 len. 4]: CHAPTER TWENTY ONE .
Parsing [sent. 4198 len. 18]: The winter set in very severe , and the falls of snow were very heavy and frequent .

Parsing [sent. 4209 len. 56]: There was one thing , however , that Humphrey had not forgotten , which was , that he had told Edward that he would try and capture some of the forest ponies ; and during the whole of the time since the heavy fall of snow had taken place he had been making his arrangements .

Parsing [sent. 4213 len. 65]: About three miles from the cottage he found what he thought would suit him ; there was a sort of avenue between two thickets , about a hundred yards wide ; and the wind blowing through this avenue , during the snow-storm , had drifted the snow at one end of it , and raised right across it a large mound several feet high .
Parsing [sent. 4214 len. 44]: By strewing small bundles of hay he drew the herd of ponies into this avenue , and in it he left them a good quantity to feed upon every night for several nights , till at last the herd went there every morning .
Parsing [sent. 4215 len. 15]: `` Now , Pablo , we must make a trial , '' said Humphrey .
Parsing [sent. 4216 len. 15]: `` You must get your lassos ready , in case they should be required .

Parsing [sent. 4220 len. 17]: `` I see , '' said Pablo ; `` very good -- soon catch them . ''

Parsing [sent. 4224 len. 37]: About daylight the herd came to pick up the hay as usual , and after they had passed them Humphrey and Pablo followed in the thicket , not wishing to show themselves till the last moment .
Parsing [sent. 4225 len. 32]: While the ponies were busy with the hay , they suddenly ran out into the avenue and separated , so as to prevent the ponies from attempting to gallop past them .
Parsing [sent. 4226 len. 136]: Shouting as loud as they could , as they ran up to the ponies , and calling to the dogs , who immediately set up a barking on each side ; the ponies , alarmed at the noise and the appearance of humphrey and Pablo , naturally set off in the only direction which appeared to them to be clear , and galloped away towards the mound of drifted snow , with their tails streaming , and snorting and plunging in the snow as they hurried along ; but as soon as they arrived at the mound they plunged first up to their bellies , and afterwards , as they attempted to force their way where the snow was deeper , many of them stuck fast altogether , and struggled to clear themselves in vain .
Parsing [sent. 4227 len. 48]: Humphrey and Pablo , who had followed them as fast as they could run , now came up with them and threw the lasso over the neck of one , and ropes with slip nooses over two more , which were floundering in the snow there together .

Parsing [sent. 4362 len. 30]: The following morning he examined the letters which had been given to him by the Intendant , and inquired of Sampson if he could direct him on his way .
Parsing [sent. 4363 len. 34]: Sampson knew London well : and Edward set out to Spring Gardens to deliver a letter , which the intendant informed him was confidential , to a person of the name of Langton .
Parsing [sent. 4364 len. 47]: Edward knocked and was ushered in , Sampson taking a seat in the hall while Edward was shown into a handsomely-furnished library , where he found himself in the presence of a tall spare man , dressed after the fashion of the Roundheads of the time .

Parsing [sent. 4366 len. 27]: Mr Langton bowed and requested Edward to sit down ; and after Edward had taken a chair , he then seated himself and opened the letter .
Parsing [sent. 4367 len. 39]: `` You are right welcome , Master Armitage , '' said Mr Langton ; `` I find that , young as you appear to be , you are in the whole confidence of our mutual friend Mr Heatherstone .
Parsing [sent. 4368 len. 37]: He hints at your being probably obliged to take a journey to the north , and that you will be glad to take charge of any letters which I may have to send in that direction .

Parsing [sent. 4370 len. 12]: How wears our good friend Heatherstone , and his daughter ? ''
Parsing [sent. 4371 len. 7]: `` Quite well , sir . ''

Parsing [sent. 4373 len. 6]: Is it not so ? ''
Parsing [sent. 4374 len. 22]: `` It is , Mr Langton ; and as gentle and pretty a child as you could wish to see . ''

Parsing [sent. 4376 len. 7]: `` Yesterday evening , sir . ''
Parsing [sent. 4377 len. 9]: `` And do you propose any stay ? ''
Parsing [sent. 4378 len. 17]: `` That I can not answer , sir ; I must be guided by your advice .

Parsing [sent. 4380 len. 71]: `` It is my opinion , Master Armitage , that the less you are seen in this city the better ; there are hundreds employed to find out newcomers , and to discover from their people , or by other means , for what purpose they may have come ; for you must be aware , Master Armitage , that the times are dangerous and people 's minds are various .
Parsing [sent. 4381 len. 27]: In attempting to free ourselves from what we considered despotism , we have created for ourselves a worse despotism , and one that is less endurable .
Parsing [sent. 4382 len. 24]: It is to be hoped that what has passed will make not only kings , but subjects , wiser than they have been .
Parsing [sent. 4383 len. 12]: Now what do you propose -- to leave this instantly ? ''
Parsing [sent. 4384 len. 10]: `` Certainly , if you think it advisable . ''
Parsing [sent. 4385 len. 12]: `` My advice , then , is to leave London immediately .
Parsing [sent. 4386 len. 31]: I will give you letters to some friends of mine in Lancashire and Yorkshire ; in either county you can remain unnoticed , and make what preparations you think necessary .
Parsing [sent. 4387 len. 33]: But do nothing in haste -- consult well , and be guided by them , who will , if it is considered advisable and prudent , join with you in your project .
Parsing [sent. 4388 len. 6]: I need say no more .
Parsing [sent. 4389 len. 20]: Call upon me to-morrow morning an hour before noon , and I will have letters ready for you . ''
Parsing [sent. 4390 len. 13]: Edward rose to depart , and thanked Mr Langton for his kindness .
Parsing [sent. 4391 len. 19]: `` Farewell , Master Armitage , '' said Langton ; `` to-morrow at the hour of eleven ! ''
Parsing [sent. 4392 len. 66]: Edward then quitted the house , and delivered the other letters of credence , the only one of importance at the moment was the one of credit ; the others were to various members of the Parliament , desiring them to know Master Armitage as a confidential friend of the Intendant , and in case of need to exert their good offices in his behalf .
Parsing [sent. 4393 len. 18]: The letter of credit was upon a Hamburgh merchant , who asked Edward if he required money .
Parsing [sent. 4394 len. 42]: Edward replied that he did not at present , but that he had business to do for his employer in the north , and might require some when there , if it was possible to obtain it so far from London .
Parsing [sent. 4395 len. 7]: `` When do you set out ?

Parsing [sent. 4399 len. 7]: Edward then returned to the hotel .
Parsing [sent. 4400 len. 66]: Before he went to bed he told Sampson that he found that he had to leave London on Mr Heatherstone 's affairs , and might be absent some time ; he concluded by observing that he did not consider it necessary to take him with him , as he could dispense with his services , and Mr Heatherstone would be glad to have him back .

Parsing [sent. 4416 len. 4]: You understand me .

Parsing [sent. 4418 len. 13]: You of course are going down to your relations in the north .
Parsing [sent. 4419 len. 5]: Have you pistols ? ''

Parsing [sent. 4421 len. 30]: `` Then they are good ones , I 'll answer for it : no man was more particular about his weapons , or knew how to use them better .
Parsing [sent. 4422 len. 12]: Farewell , Master Armitage , and may success attend you . ''
Parsing [sent. 4423 len. 15]: Mr Langton held out his hand to Edward , who respectfully took his leave .
Parsing [sent. 4424 len. 4]: CHAPTER TWENTY TWO .
Parsing [sent. 4425 len. 26]: Edward was certain that Mr Langton would not have advised him to leave London if he had not considered that it was dangerous to remain .
Parsing [sent. 4426 len. 61]: He therefore first called upon the Hamburgh merchant , who , upon his explanation , gave him a letter of credit to a friend who resided in the city of York ; and then returned to the hotel , packed up his saddle-bags , paid his reckoning , and , mounting his horse , set off on the northern road .
Parsing [sent. 4427 len. 31]: As it was late in the afternoon before he was clear of the metropolis , he did not proceed farther than Barnet , where he pulled up at the inn .
Parsing [sent. 4428 len. 33]: As soon as he had seen his horse attended to , Edward , with his saddle-bags on his arm , went into the room in the inn where all the travellers congregated .
Parsing [sent. 4429 len. 35]: Having procured a bed and given his saddle-bags into the charge of the hostess , he sat down by the fire , which , although it was warm weather , was nevertheless kept alight .
Parsing [sent. 4430 len. 24]: Edward had made no alteration in the dress which he had worn since he had been received in the house of Mr Heatherstone .
Parsing [sent. 4431 len. 9]: It was plain , although of good materials .
Parsing [sent. 4432 len. 24]: He wore a high-crowned hat , and altogether would , from his attire , have been taken for one of the Roundhead party .
Parsing [sent. 4433 len. 25]: His sword and shoulder-belt were indeed of more gay appearance than those usually worn by the Roundheads ; but this was the only difference .
Parsing [sent. 4434 len. 20]: When Edward first entered the room there were three persons in it , whose appearance was not very prepossessing .
Parsing [sent. 4435 len. 28]: They were dressed in what had once been very gay attire , but which now exhibited tarnished lace , stains of wine , and dust from travelling .
Parsing [sent. 4436 len. 28]: They eyed him as he entered with his saddle-bags , and one of them said -- `` That 's a fine horse you were riding , sir .
Parsing [sent. 4437 len. 6]: Has he much speed ? ''
Parsing [sent. 4438 len. 32]: `` He has , '' replied Edward , as he turned away , and went into the bar to speak with the hostess , and give his property into her care .
Parsing [sent. 4439 len. 7]: `` Going north , sir ? ''
Parsing [sent. 4440 len. 8]: inquired the same person when Edward returned .
Parsing [sent. 4441 len. 17]: `` Not exactly , '' replied Edward , walking to the window to avoid further conversation .
Parsing [sent. 4442 len. 15]: `` The Roundhead is on the stilts , '' observed another of the party .
Parsing [sent. 4443 len. 37]: `` Yes , '' replied the first ; `` it is easy to see that he has not been accustomed to be addressed by gentlemen ; for half a pin I would slit his ears . ''
Parsing [sent. 4444 len. 19]: Edward did not choose to reply ; he folded his arms and looked at the man with contempt .
Parsing [sent. 4445 len. 38]: The hostess , who had overheard the conversation , now called for her husband , and desired him to go into the room and prevent any further insults to the young gentleman who had just come in .
Parsing [sent. 4446 len. 53]: The host , who knew the parties , entered the room , and said -- `` Now you 'll clear out of this as fast as you can ; be off with you , and go to the stables , or I 'll send for somebody whom you will not like . ''
Parsing [sent. 4447 len. 19]: The three men rose and swaggered , but obeyed the host 's orders , and left the room .
Parsing [sent. 4448 len. 25]: `` I am sorry , young Master , that these roysterers should have affronted you , as my wife tells me that they have .
Parsing [sent. 4449 len. 11]: I did not know that they were in the house .

Parsing [sent. 4451 len. 30]: `` Thank you for your caution , my good host , '' replied Edward ; `` I thought that they were highwaymen , or something of that sort . ''
Parsing [sent. 4452 len. 26]: `` You have made a good guess , sir ; but nothing has yet been proved against them , or they would not be here .
Parsing [sent. 4453 len. 16]: In these times we have strange customers , and hardly know who we take in .
Parsing [sent. 4454 len. 24]: You have a good sword there , sir , I have no doubt ; but I trust that you have other arms . ''

Parsing [sent. 4456 len. 7]: `` That 's right , sir .
Parsing [sent. 4457 len. 11]: Will you take anything before you go to bed ? ''
Parsing [sent. 4458 len. 21]: `` Indeed I will , for I am hungry ; anything will do , with a pint of wine . ''
Parsing [sent. 4459 len. 21]: As soon as he had supped Edward asked the hostess for his saddle-bags , and went up to his bed .
Parsing [sent. 4460 len. 17]: Early the next morning he rose and went to the stable to see his horse fed .
Parsing [sent. 4461 len. 17]: The three men were in the stables , but they did not say anything to him .
Parsing [sent. 4462 len. 28]: Edward returned to the inn , called for breakfast , and , as soon as he had finished , took out his pistols to renew the priming .
Parsing [sent. 4463 len. 25]: While so occupied he happened to look up , and perceived one of the men with his face against the window , watching him .
Parsing [sent. 4464 len. 36]: `` Well , now you see what you have to expect , if you try your trade with me , '' thought Edward , `` I am very glad that you have been spying . ''
Parsing [sent. 4465 len. 28]: Having replaced his pistols , Edward paid his reckoning , and went to the stable desiring the ostler to saddle his horse and fix on his saddle-bags .
Parsing [sent. 4466 len. 12]: As soon as this was done he mounted and rode off .
Parsing [sent. 4467 len. 19]: Before he was well clear of the town the highwaymen cantered past him on three well-bred active horses .
Parsing [sent. 4468 len. 49]: `` I presume we shall meet again , '' thought Edward , who for some time cantered at a gentle rate , and then , as his horse was very fresh , he put him to a faster pace , intending to do a long day 's work .
Parsing [sent. 4469 len. 59]: He had ridden about fifteen miles , when he came to a heath , and , as he continued at a fast trot , he perceived the three highwaymen about a quarter of a mile in advance of him ; they were descending a hill which was between them , and he soon lost sight of them again .
Parsing [sent. 4470 len. 21]: Edward now pulled up his horse to let him recover his wind , and walked him gently up the hill .
Parsing [sent. 4471 len. 34]: He had nearly gained the summit when he heard the report of firearms , and soon afterwards a man on horse back , in full speed , galloped over the hill towards him .
Parsing [sent. 4472 len. 14]: He had a pistol in his hand , and his head turned back .
Parsing [sent. 4473 len. 19]: The reason for this was soon evident , as immediately after him appeared the three highwaymen in pursuit .
Parsing [sent. 4474 len. 14]: One fired his pistol at the man who fled , and missed him .
Parsing [sent. 4475 len. 19]: The man then fired in return , and with true aim , as one of the highwaymen fell .
Parsing [sent. 4476 len. 32]: All this was so sudden that Edward had hardly time to draw his pistol and put spurs to his horse before the parties were upon him , and were passing him .
Parsing [sent. 4477 len. 16]: Edward levelled at the second highwayman as he passed him , and the man fell .
Parsing [sent. 4478 len. 28]: The third highwayman , perceiving this , turned his horse to the side of the road , cleared a ditch , and galloped away across the heath .
Parsing [sent. 4479 len. 51]: The man who had been attacked had pulled up his horse when Edward came to his assistance , and now rode up to him , saying -- `` I have to thank you , sir , for your timely aid ; for these rascals were too many for me . ''
Parsing [sent. 4480 len. 12]: `` You are not hurt , I trust , sir ? ''
Parsing [sent. 4481 len. 3]: replied Edward .

Parsing [sent. 4483 len. 10]: They attacked me but half a mile from here .
Parsing [sent. 4484 len. 49]: I was proceeding north when I heard the clatter of hoofs behind me ; I looked round , and saw at once what they were , and I sprung my horse out of the road to a thicket close to it , that they might not surround me .

Parsing [sent. 4487 len. 5]: The result you saw .
Parsing [sent. 4488 len. 21]: Between us we have broken up the gang ; for both these fellows seem dead , or nearly so . ''
Parsing [sent. 4489 len. 9]: `` What shall we do with them ? ''

Parsing [sent. 4492 len. 23]: I have important business at the city of York , and can not waste my time in depositions , and such nonsense .
Parsing [sent. 4493 len. 20]: It is only two scoundrels less in the world , and there 's an end of the matter . ''
Parsing [sent. 4494 len. 23]: As Edward was equally anxious to proceed , he agreed with the stranger that it was best to do as he proposed .
Parsing [sent. 4495 len. 25]: `` I am also going north , '' replied Edward , `` and am anxious to get there as soon as I can . ''
Parsing [sent. 4496 len. 14]: `` With your permission we will ride together , '' said the stranger .

Parsing [sent. 4498 len. 31]: There was such a gentlemanlike , frank , and courteous air about the stranger , that Edward immediately assented to his proposal of their riding in company for mutual protection .
Parsing [sent. 4499 len. 42]: He was a powerful , well-made man , of apparently about one or two and twenty , remarkably handsome in person , dressed richly , but not gaudily , in the cavalier fashion , and wore a hat with a feather .
Parsing [sent. 4500 len. 29]: As they proceeded , they entered into conversation on indifferent matters for some time , neither party attempting by any question to discover who his companion might be .
Parsing [sent. 4501 len. 45]: Edward had more than once , when the conversation flagged for a minute , considered what reply he should give in case his companion should ask him the cause of his journey , and at last had made up his mind what to say .
Parsing [sent. 4502 len. 80]: A little before noon they pulled up to bait their horses at a small village , -- the stranger observing that he avoided Saint Alban 's , and all other large towns , as he did not wish to satisfy the curiosity of people , or to have his motions watched ; and therefore , if Edward had no objection , he knew the country so well that he could save time by allowing him to direct their path .
Parsing [sent. 4503 len. 61]: Edward was , as may be supposed , very agreeable to this , and during their whole journey they never entered a town , except they rode through it after dark ; and put up at humble inns on the roadside , where , if not quite so well attended to , at all events they were free from observation .
Parsing [sent. 4504 len. 23]: It was , however , impossible that this reserve could continue long , as they became more and more intimate every day .
Parsing [sent. 4505 len. 35]: At last the stranger said -- `` Master Armitage , we have travelled together for some time , interchanging thoughts and feelings , but with due reserve as respects ourselves and our own plans .
Parsing [sent. 4506 len. 5]: Is this to continue ?
Parsing [sent. 4507 len. 29]: If so , of course you have but to say so ; but if you feel inclined to trust me , I have the same feeling towards you .

Parsing [sent. 4513 len. 44]: `` I believe that , '' replied Chaloner ; `` and I can not help thinking you are bound north on the same business as myself , which is , I confess to you honestly , to strike a blow for the king .
Parsing [sent. 4514 len. 38]: If you are on the same errand , I have two old relations in Lancashire who are stanch to the cause ; and I am going to their house to remain until I can join the army .
Parsing [sent. 4515 len. 26]: If you wish it , you shall come with me , and I will promise you kind treatment and safety while under their roof . ''

Parsing [sent. 4525 len. 5]: `` This is excellent !

Parsing [sent. 4569 len. 24]: Late in the evening they arrived at Portlake , a grand old mansion , situated in a park crowded with fine old timber .

Parsing [sent. 4572 len. 71]: `` And something did very nearly happen to me , '' replied Chaloner , `` had it not been for the timely assistance of my friend here , who , notwithstanding his Puritan attire , I hardly need tell you is a Cavalier devoted to the good cause , when I state that he is the son of Colonel Beverley , who fell at Naseby with my good father . ''
Parsing [sent. 4573 len. 23]: `` No one can be more welcome , then , '' replied the old ladies , who extended their hands to Edward .

Parsing [sent. 4578 len. 26]: `` Not so ; let me have the letters ; we can read them before supper , and talk them over when at table . ''
Parsing [sent. 4579 len. 24]: One of the ladies produced the letters , which Chaloner , as he read them , handed over to Edward for his perusal .
Parsing [sent. 4580 len. 38]: They were from General Middleton , and some other friends of Chaloner 's who were with the army , giving him information as to what was going on , and what their prospects were supposed to be .
Parsing [sent. 4581 len. 35]: `` You see that they have marched already , '' said Chaloner , `` and I think the plan is a good one , and it has put General Cromwell in an awkward position .
Parsing [sent. 4582 len. 16]: Our army is now between his and London , with three days march in advance .
Parsing [sent. 4583 len. 25]: And we shall now be able to pick up our English adherents , who can join us without risk , as we go along .
Parsing [sent. 4584 len. 27]: It has been a bold step , but a good one ; and if they only continue as well as they have begun we shall succeed .
Parsing [sent. 4585 len. 23]: The Parliamentary army is not equal to ours in numbers , as it is ; and we shall add to ours daily .
Parsing [sent. 4586 len. 23]: The king has sent to the Isle of Man for the Earl of Derby , who is expected to join to-morrow . ''
Parsing [sent. 4587 len. 11]: `` And where is the army at this moment ? ''
Parsing [sent. 4588 len. 3]: inquired Edward .
Parsing [sent. 4589 len. 27]: `` They will be but a few miles from us to-night , their march is so rapid ; to-morrow we will join if it pleases . ''
Parsing [sent. 4590 len. 8]: `` Most willingly , '' replied Edward .
Parsing [sent. 4591 len. 20]: After an hour 's more conversation , they were shown into their rooms , and retired for the night .
Parsing [sent. 4592 len. 4]: CHAPTER TWENTY THREE .
Parsing [sent. 4593 len. 42]: The next morning , before they had quitted their beds , a messenger arrived with letters from General Middleton , and from him they found that the king 's army had encamped on the evening before not six miles from Portlake .
Parsing [sent. 4594 len. 62]: As they hastily dressed themselves , Chaloner proposed to Edward that a little alteration in his dress would be necessary ; and taking him to a wardrobe in which had been put aside some suits of his own , worn when he was a younger and slighter-made man than he now was , he requested Edward to make use of them .
Parsing [sent. 4595 len. 47]: Edward , who was aware that Chaloner was right in his proposal , selected two suits of colours which pleased him most ; and dressing in one , and changing his hat for one more befitting his new attire , was transformed into a handsome Cavalier .
Parsing [sent. 4596 len. 28]: As soon as they had broken their fast they took leave of the old ladies , and , mounting their horses , set off for the camp .
Parsing [sent. 4597 len. 62]: An hour 's ride brought them to the outposts ; and communicating with the officer on duty , they were conducted by an orderly to the tent of General Middleton , who received Chaloner with great warmth as an old friend , and was very courteous to Edward as soon as he heard that he was the son of Colonel Beverley .
Parsing [sent. 4598 len. 60]: `` I have wanted you , Chaloner , '' said Middleton ; `` we are raising a troop of horse ; the Duke of Buckingham commands it , but Massey will be the real leader of it ; you have influence in this county , and will , I have no doubt , bring us many good hands . ''
Parsing [sent. 4599 len. 9]: `` Where is the Earl of Derby ? ''
Parsing [sent. 4600 len. 24]: `` Joined us this morning ; we have marched so quick that we have not had time to pick our adherents up . ''
Parsing [sent. 4601 len. 6]: `` And General Lesley ? ''
Parsing [sent. 4602 len. 14]: `` Is by no means in good spirits : why I know not .
Parsing [sent. 4603 len. 25]: We have too many ministers with his army , that is certain , and they do harm ; but we can not help ourselves .
Parsing [sent. 4604 len. 30]: His majesty must be visible by this time ; if you are ready I will introduce you ; and when that is done we will talk matters over . ''
Parsing [sent. 4605 len. 39]: General Middleton then walked with them to the house in which the king had taken up his quarters for the night ; and after a few minutes ' waiting in the anteroom they were admitted into his presence .
Parsing [sent. 4606 len. 36]: `` Allow me , your majesty , '' said General Middleton , after the first salutations , `` to present to you Major Chaloner , whose father 's name is not unknown to you . ''
Parsing [sent. 4607 len. 28]: `` On the contrary , well-known to us , '' replied the king , `` as a loyal and faithful subject , whose loss we must deplore .
Parsing [sent. 4608 len. 15]: I have no doubt that his son inherits his courage and his fidelity . ''
Parsing [sent. 4609 len. 16]: The king held out his hand , and Chaloner bent his knee and kissed it .
Parsing [sent. 4610 len. 32]: `` And now , your majesty will be surprised that I should present to you one of a house supposed to be extinct -- the eldest son of Colonel Beverley . ''
Parsing [sent. 4611 len. 4]: `` Indeed ! ''
Parsing [sent. 4612 len. 19]: replied his majesty ; `` I heard that all his family perished at the ruthless burning of Arnwood .
Parsing [sent. 4613 len. 26]: I hold myself fortunate , as a king , that even one son of so loyal and brave a gentleman as Colonel Beverley has escaped .
Parsing [sent. 4614 len. 35]: You are welcome , young sir -- most welcome to us ; you must be near us ; the very name of Beverley will be pleasing to our ears by night or day . ''
Parsing [sent. 4615 len. 24]: Edward knelt down and kissed his majesty 's hand , and the king said -- `` What can we do for a Beverley ?
Parsing [sent. 4616 len. 17]: Let us know , that we may show our feelings towards his father 's memory ? ''
Parsing [sent. 4617 len. 26]: `` All I request is , that your majesty will allow me to be near you in the hour of danger , '' replied Edward .
Parsing [sent. 4618 len. 23]: `` A right Beverley reply , '' said the king , `` and so we shall see to it , Middleton . ''
Parsing [sent. 4619 len. 30]: After a few more courteous words from his majesty they withdrew ; but General Middleton was recalled by the king for a minute or two to receive his commands .
Parsing [sent. 4620 len. 65]: When he rejoined Edward and Chaloner , he said to Edward -- `` I have orders to send in for his majesty 's signature your commission as captain of horse , and attached to the king 's personal staff ; it is a high compliment to the memory of your father , sir , and , I may add , your own personal appearance .
Parsing [sent. 4621 len. 34]: Chaloner will see to your uniforms and accoutrements ; you are well mounted , I believe you have no time to lose , as we march to-morrow for Warrington , in Cheshire . ''
Parsing [sent. 4622 len. 11]: `` Has anything been heard of the Parliamentary army ? ''
Parsing [sent. 4623 len. 24]: `` Yes ; they are on the march towards London by the Yorkshire road , intending to cut us off if they can .
Parsing [sent. 4624 len. 19]: And now , gentlemen , farewell ; for I have no idle time , I assure you . ''
Parsing [sent. 4625 len. 12]: Edward was soon equipped , and now attended upon the king .
Parsing [sent. 4626 len. 19]: When they arrived at Warrington they found a body of horse drawn up to oppose their passage onwards .
Parsing [sent. 4627 len. 82]: These were charged , and fled with a trifling loss ; and as they were known to be commanded by Lambert , one of Cromwell 's best generals , there was great exultation in the king 's army ; but the fact was that Lambert had acted upon Cromwell 's orders , which were , to harass and delay the march of the king as much as possible , but not to risk with his small force anything like an engagement .
Parsing [sent. 4628 len. 38]: After this skirmish it was considered advisable to send back the Earl of Derby and many other officers of importance into Lancashire , that they might collect the king 's adherents in that quarter and in Cheshire .
Parsing [sent. 4629 len. 19]: Accordingly the earl , with about two hundred officers and gentlemen , left the army with that intention .
Parsing [sent. 4630 len. 85]: It was then considered that it would be advisable to march the army direct to London ; but the men were so fatigued with the rapidity of the march up to the present time , and the weather was so warm , that it was decided in the negative ; and as Worcester was a town well affected to the king , and the country abounded with provisions , it was resolved that the army should march there , and wait for English reinforcements .
Parsing [sent. 4631 len. 25]: This was done ; the city opened the gates with every mark of satisfaction , and supplied the army with all that it required .
Parsing [sent. 4632 len. 56]: The first bad news which reached them was the dispersion and defeat of the whole of the Earl of Derby 's party by a regiment of militia , which had surprised them at Wigan during the night , when they were all asleep , and had no idea that any enemy was near to them .
Parsing [sent. 4633 len. 35]: Although attacked at such a disadvantage , they defended themselves till a large portion of them were killed , and the remainder were taken prisoners , and most of them brutally put to death .
Parsing [sent. 4634 len. 18]: The Earl of Derby was made a prisoner , but not put to death with the others .
Parsing [sent. 4635 len. 12]: `` This is bad news , Chaloner , '' said Edward .
Parsing [sent. 4636 len. 53]: `` Yes ; it is more than bad , '' replied the latter ; `` we have lost our best officers , who never should have left the army ; and now , the consequences of the defeat will be that we shall not have any people coming forward to join us .
Parsing [sent. 4637 len. 47]: The winning side is the right side in this world ; and there is more evil than that : the Duke of Buckingham has claimed the command of the army , which the king has refused ; so that we are beginning to fight among ourselves .
Parsing [sent. 4638 len. 13]: General Lesley is evidently dispirited , and thinks bad of the cause .
Parsing [sent. 4639 len. 10]: Middleton is the only man who does his duty .
Parsing [sent. 4640 len. 39]: Depend upon it we shall have Cromwell upon us before we are aware of it ; and we are in a state of sad confusion -- officers quarrelling , men disobedient , much talking , and little doing .
Parsing [sent. 4641 len. 27]: Here we have been five days , and the works , which have been proposed to be thrown up as defences , not yet begun . ''
Parsing [sent. 4642 len. 22]: `` I can not but admire the patience of the king , with so much to harass and annoy him . ''
Parsing [sent. 4643 len. 42]: `` He must be patient , perforce , '' replied Chaloner ; `` he plays for a crown , and it is a high stake ; but he can not command the minds of men , although he may the persons .
Parsing [sent. 4644 len. 35]: I am no croaker , Beverley ; but this I do say , that if we succeed with this army , as it is at present disorganised , we shall perform a miracle . ''
Parsing [sent. 4645 len. 54]: `` We must hope for the best , '' replied Edward ; `` common danger may cement those who would otherwise be asunder ; and when they have the army of Cromwell before them , they may be induced to forget their private quarrels and jealousies and unite in the good cause . ''
Parsing [sent. 4646 len. 35]: `` I wish I could be of your opinion , Beverley , '' replied Chaloner ; `` but I have mixed with the world longer than you have , and I think otherwise . ''
Parsing [sent. 4647 len. 69]: Several more days passed , during which no defences were thrown up , and the confusion and quarrelling in the army continued to increase , until at last news arrived that Cromwell was within half a day 's march of them , and that he had collected all the militia on his route , and was now in numbers nearly double to those in the king 's army .
Parsing [sent. 4648 len. 32]: All was amazement and confusion -- nothing had been done -- no arrangements had been made -- and Chaloner told Edward that all was lost if immediate steps were not taken .
Parsing [sent. 4649 len. 13]: On the 3rd of October the army of Cromwell appeared in sight .
Parsing [sent. 4650 len. 54]: Edward had been on horseback , attending the king , for the best part of the night ; the disposition of the troops had been made as well as it could ; and it was concluded , as Cromwell 's army remained quiet , that no attempt would be made on that day .
Parsing [sent. 4651 len. 17]: About noon the king returned to his lodging , to take some refreshment after his fatigue .
Parsing [sent. 4652 len. 20]: Edward was with him ; but before an hour had passed the alarm came that the armies were engaged .
Parsing [sent. 4653 len. 54]: The king mounted his horse , which was ready saddled at the door ; but before he could ride out of the city he was met and nearly beaten back by the whole body almost of his own cavalry , who came running on with such force that he could not stop them .
Parsing [sent. 4654 len. 44]: His majesty called to several of the officers by name , but they paid no attention ; and so great was the panic , that both the king , and his staff who attended him , were nearly overthrown and trampled under foot .
Parsing [sent. 4655 len. 34]: Cromwell had passed a large portion of his troops over the river without the knowledge of his opponents , and when the attack was made in so unexpected a quarter a panic ensued .
Parsing [sent. 4656 len. 77]: Where General Middleton and the Duke of Hamilton commanded , a very brave resistance was made ; but Middleton being wounded , and the Duke of Hamilton having had his leg taken off by a round shot , and many gentlemen having fallen , the troops , deserted by the remainder of the army , at last gave way , and the rout was general , the foot throwing away their muskets before they were discharged .
Parsing [sent. 4657 len. 25]: His majesty rode back into the town and found a body of horse , who had been persuaded by Chaloner to make a stand .
Parsing [sent. 4658 len. 19]: `` Follow me , '' said his majesty , `` we will see what the enemy are about .
Parsing [sent. 4659 len. 21]: I do not think they pursue , and if so , we may yet rally from this foolish panic . ''
Parsing [sent. 4660 len. 61]: His majesty , followed by Edward , Chaloner , and several of his personal staff , then galloped out to reconnoitre ; but to his mortification he found that the troops had not followed him , but gone out of the town by the other gate , and that the enemy 's cavalry in pursuit were actually in the town .
Parsing [sent. 4661 len. 30]: Under such circumstances , by the advice of Chaloner and Edward , his majesty withdrew , and turning his horse 's head he made all haste to leave Worcester .
Parsing [sent. 4662 len. 52]: After several hours ' riding , the king found himself in company of about 4000 of the cavalry who had so disgracefully fled ; but they were still so panic-struck that he could put no confidence in them , and having advised with those about him he resolved to quit them .
Parsing [sent. 4663 len. 53]: This he did without mentioning his intentions to any of his staff , not even Chaloner or Edward , leaving at night with two of his servants , whom he dismissed as soon as it was daylight , considering that his chance of escape would be greater if he was quite alone .
Parsing [sent. 4664 len. 43]: It was not till next morning that the troops discovered that the king had left them , and then they determined to separate , and as the major portion were from Scotland to make what haste they could back to that country .
Parsing [sent. 4665 len. 11]: And now Chaloner and Edward consulted as to their plans .
Parsing [sent. 4666 len. 52]: `` It appears to me , '' said Edward , laughing , `` that the danger of this campaign of ours will consist in getting back again to our own homes ; for I can most safely assert that I have not as yet struck a blow for the king . ''
Parsing [sent. 4667 len. 8]: `` That is true enough , Beverley .
Parsing [sent. 4668 len. 11]: When do you purpose going back to the New Forest ?
Parsing [sent. 4669 len. 18]: I think , if you will permit me , I will accompany you , '' said Chaloner .
Parsing [sent. 4670 len. 18]: `` All the pursuit will be to the northward to intercept and overtake the retreat into Scotland .
Parsing [sent. 4671 len. 28]: I can not therefore go to Lancashire ; and indeed , as they know that I am out , they will be looking for me everywhere . ''
Parsing [sent. 4672 len. 24]: `` Then come with me , '' said Edward ; `` I will find you protection till you can decide what to do .
Parsing [sent. 4673 len. 47]: Let us ride on away from this , and we will talk over the matter as we go ; but depend upon it , the farther south we get the safer we shall be ; but still not safe , unless we can change our costume .
Parsing [sent. 4674 len. 27]: There will be a strict search for the king to the south , as they will presume that he will try to get safe to France .
Parsing [sent. 4675 len. 2]: Hark !
Parsing [sent. 4676 len. 4]: What is that ?
Parsing [sent. 4677 len. 7]: I heard the report of arms .
Parsing [sent. 4678 len. 14]: Let us ride up this hill and see what is going on . ''
Parsing [sent. 4679 len. 31]: They did so , and perceived that there was a skirmish between a party of Cavaliers and some of the Parliamentary cavalry at about a quarter of a mile distant .
Parsing [sent. 4680 len. 18]: `` Come , Chaloner , let us at all events have one blow , '' said Edward .
Parsing [sent. 4681 len. 39]: `` Agreed , '' replied Chaloner , spurring his horse ; and down they went at full speed , and in a minute were in the _ melee _ , coming on the rear of the Parliamentary troops .
Parsing [sent. 4682 len. 9]: This sudden attack from behind decided the affair .
Parsing [sent. 4683 len. 34]: The Parliamentary troopers , thinking that there were wore than two coming upon them , made off after another minute 's combat , leaving five or six of their men on the ground .
Parsing [sent. 4684 len. 9]: `` Thanks , Chaloner thanks , Beverley ! ''
Parsing [sent. 4685 len. 9]: said a voice , which they immediately recognised .
Parsing [sent. 4686 len. 11]: It was that of one of the king 's pages .
Parsing [sent. 4687 len. 19]: `` These fellows with me were just about to run if you had not come to our aid .
Parsing [sent. 4688 len. 18]: I will remain with them no longer , but join you if you will permit me . ''
Parsing [sent. 4689 len. 18]: `` At all events remain here till they go away -- I will send them off . ''
Parsing [sent. 4690 len. 18]: `` My lads , you must all separate , or there will be no chance of escape .
Parsing [sent. 4691 len. 8]: No more than two should ride together .
Parsing [sent. 4692 len. 14]: Depend upon it we shall have more of the troops here directly . ''
Parsing [sent. 4693 len. 53]: The men , about fifteen in number , who had been in company with Grenville , considered that Chaloner 's advice was good , and without ceremony set off with their horses ' heads to the northward , leaving Chaloner , Edward , and Grenville together on the field of the affray .
Parsing [sent. 4694 len. 34]: A dozen men were lying on the ground , either dead or severely wounded ; seven of them were of the king 's party , and the other five of the Parliamentary troops .
Parsing [sent. 4695 len. 53]: `` Now what I propose , '' said Edward , `` is this , -- let us do what we can for those who are wounded , and then strip off the dresses and accoutrements of those Parliamentary dragoons who are dead , and dress ourselves in them , accoutrements and all .
Parsing [sent. 4696 len. 27]: We can then pass through the country in safety , as we shall be supposed to be one of the parties looking for the king . ''
Parsing [sent. 4697 len. 22]: `` That is a good idea , '' replied Chaloner , `` and the sooner it is done the better . ''
Parsing [sent. 4698 len. 68]: `` Well , '' said Edward , wiping his sword , which he still held drawn , and then sheathing it , `` I will take the spoils of this fellow nearest to me : he fell by my hand , and I am entitled to them by all the laws of war and chivalry ; but first let us dismount and look to the wounded . ''
Parsing [sent. 4699 len. 60]: They tied their horses to a tree , and having given what assistance they could to the wounded men , they proceeded to strip three of the Parliamentary troopers ; and then , laying aside their own habiliments , they dressed themselves in the uniform of the enemy , and mounting their horses made all haste from the place .
Parsing [sent. 4700 len. 17]: Having gained about twelve miles , they pulled up and rode at a more leisurely pace .
Parsing [sent. 4701 len. 46]: It was now eight o'clock in the evening , but still not very dark ; they therefore rode on another five miles , till they came to a small village , where they dismounted at an ale-house , and put their horses into the stable .
Parsing [sent. 4702 len. 18]: `` We must be insolent and brutal in our manners , or we shall be suspected . ''
Parsing [sent. 4703 len. 28]: `` Very true , '' said Grenville , giving the ostler a kick and telling him to bestir himself if he did not want his ears cropped .
Parsing [sent. 4704 len. 16]: They entered the ale-house , and soon found out they were held in great terror .
Parsing [sent. 4705 len. 66]: They ordered everything of the best to be produced , and threatened to set fire to the house if it was not ; they turned the man and his wife out of their bed , and all three went to sleep in it ; and , in short , they behaved in such an arbitrary manner that nobody doubted that they were Cromwell 's men .
Parsing [sent. 4706 len. 30]: In the morning they set off again , by Chaloner 's advice paying for nothing that they had ordered , although they had all of them plenty of money .
Parsing [sent. 4707 len. 41]: They now rode fast , inquiring at the places which they passed through whether any fugitives had been seen , and if they came to a town , inquiring , before they entered , whether there were any Parliamentary troops .
Parsing [sent. 4708 len. 53]: So well did they manage , that after four days they had gained the skirts of the New Forest , and concealed themselves in a thicket till night-time , when Edward proposed that he should conduct his fellow-travellers to the cottage , where he would leave them till his plans were arranged .
Parsing [sent. 4709 len. 7]: Edward had already arranged his plans .
Parsing [sent. 4710 len. 47]: His great object was to ward off any suspicion of where he had been , and of course any idea that the Intendant had been a party to his acts ; and the fortunate change of his dress enabled him now to do so with success .
Parsing [sent. 4711 len. 69]: He had decided to conduct his two friends to the cottage that night , and the next morning to ride over in his Parliamentary costume to the Intendant 's house , and bring the first news of the success of Cromwell and the defeat at Worcester ; by which stratagem it would appear as if he had been with the Parliamentary , and not with the Royalist army .
Parsing [sent. 4712 len. 61]: As they had travelled along , they found that the news of Cromwell 's success had not yet arrived : in those times there was not the rapidity of communication that we now have , and Edward thought it very probable that he would be the first to communicate the intelligence to the Intendant and those who resided near him .
Parsing [sent. 4713 len. 25]: As soon as it was dusk the three travellers left their retreat , and , guided by Edward , soon arrived at the cottage .
Parsing [sent. 4714 len. 46]: Their appearance at first created no little consternation , for Humphrey and Pablo happened to be in the yard when they heard the clattering of the swords and accoutrements , and through the gloom observed , as they advanced , that the party were troopers .
Parsing [sent. 4715 len. 64]: At first Humphrey was for running on and barring the door , but , on a second reflection , he felt that he could not do a more imprudent thing , if there was danger ; and he therefore contented himself with hastily imparting the intelligence to his sisters , and then remaining at the threshold to meet the coming of the parties .
Parsing [sent. 4716 len. 27]: The voice of Edward calling him by name dissipated all alarm , and in another minute he was in the arms of his brother and sisters .
Parsing [sent. 4717 len. 55]: `` First let us take our horses to the stable , Humphrey , '' said Edward , after the first greeting was over , `` and then we will come and partake of anything that Alice can prepare for us , for we have not fared over well for the last three days . ''
Parsing [sent. 4718 len. 49]: Accompanied by Humphrey and Pablo , they all went to the stables , and turned out the ponies to make room for the horses ; and as soon as they were all fed and littered down they returned to the cottage , and Chaloner and Grenville were introduced .
Parsing [sent. 4719 len. 30]: Supper was soon on the table , and they were too hungry to talk while they were eating , so but little information was gleaned from them that night .
Parsing [sent. 4720 len. 33]: However , previous to Alice and Edith leaving the room to prepare beds for the newcomers , Humphrey ascertained that all was lost , and that they had escaped from the field .
Parsing [sent. 4721 len. 29]: When the beds were ready , Chaloner and Grenville retired , and then Edward remained half an hour with Humphrey , to communicate to him what had passed .
Parsing [sent. 4722 len. 35]: Of course he could not enter into detail ; but told him that he would get information from their new guests after he had left , which he must do early in the morning .
Parsing [sent. 4723 len. 66]: `` And now , Humphrey , my advice is this : My two friends can not remain in this cottage , for many reasons ; but we have the key of Clara 's cottage , and they can take up their lodging there , and we can supply them with all they want until they find means of going abroad , which is their intention .
Parsing [sent. 4724 len. 21]: I must be off to the Intendant 's to-morrow , and the day after I will come over to you .
Parsing [sent. 4725 len. 36]: In the meantime our guests can remain here , while you and Pablo prepare the cottage for them ; and when I return everything shall be settled , and we will conduct them to it .
Parsing [sent. 4726 len. 54]: I do not think there is much danger of their being discovered while they remain there , certainly not so much as if they were here ; for we must expect parties of troops in every direction now , as they were when the king 's father made his escape from Hampton Court .
Parsing [sent. 4727 len. 31]: And now to bed , my good brother ; and call me early , for I much fear that I shall not wake up , if you do not . ''
Parsing [sent. 4728 len. 8]: The brothers then parted for the night .
Parsing [sent. 4729 len. 28]: The next morning , long before their guests were awake , Edward had been called by Humphrey , and found Pablo at the door with his horse .
Parsing [sent. 4730 len. 40]: Edward , who had put on his Parliamentary accoutrements , bade a hasty farewell to them , and set off across the forest to the house of the Intendant , where he arrived before they had left their bedrooms .
Parsing [sent. 4731 len. 18]: The first person he encountered was , very fortunately , Oswald , who was at his cottage-door .
Parsing [sent. 4732 len. 42]: Edward beckoned to him , being then about one hundred yards off ; but Oswald did not recognise him at first , and advanced towards him in a very leisurely manner , to ascertain what the trooper might wish to inquire .
Parsing [sent. 4733 len. 11]: But Edward called him Oswald , and that was sufficient .
Parsing [sent. 4734 len. 25]: In few words Edward told him how all was lost , and how he had escaped by changing clothes with one of the enemy .
Parsing [sent. 4735 len. 15]: `` I am now come to bring the news to the Intendant , Oswald .
Parsing [sent. 4736 len. 8]: You understand me , of course ? ''
Parsing [sent. 4737 len. 31]: `` Of course I do , Master Edward , and will take care that it is well-known that you have been fighting by the side of Cromwell all this time .
Parsing [sent. 4738 len. 25]: I should recommend you to show yourself in this dress for the remainder of the day , and then every one will be satisfied .
Parsing [sent. 4739 len. 11]: Shall I go to the Intendant 's before you ? ''
Parsing [sent. 4740 len. 22]: `` No , no , Oswald ; the Intendant does not require me to be introduced to him , of course .
Parsing [sent. 4741 len. 12]: I must now gallop up to his house and announce myself .
Parsing [sent. 4742 len. 14]: Farewell for the present -- I shall see you during the day . ''
Parsing [sent. 4743 len. 88]: Edward put spurs to his horse , and arrived at the Intendant 's at full speed , making no small clattering in the yard below as he went in , much to the surprise of Sampson , who came out to ascertain what was the cause , and who was not a little surprised at perceiving Edward , who threw himself off the horse , and desiring Sampson to take it to the stable , entered the kitchen , and disturbed Phoebe , who was preparing breakfast .
Parsing [sent. 4744 len. 18]: Without speaking to her , Edward passed on to the Intendant 's room , and knocked . ''
Parsing [sent. 4745 len. 7]: _ Who _ is there ? ''
Parsing [sent. 4746 len. 4]: said the Intendant .
Parsing [sent. 4747 len. 15]: `` Edward Armitage , '' was the reply , and the door was opened .
Parsing [sent. 4748 len. 15]: The Intendant started back at the sight of Edward in the trooper 's costume .

Parsing [sent. 4750 len. 8]: Sit down and tell me all . ''
Parsing [sent. 4751 len. 29]: `` All is soon told , sir , '' replied Edward , taking off his iron skull-cap , and allowing his hair to fall down on his shoulders .
Parsing [sent. 4752 len. 39]: He then , in few words , stated what had happened , and by what means he had escaped , and the reason why he had kept on the trooper 's accoutrements and made his appearance in them .
Parsing [sent. 4753 len. 48]: `` You have done very prudently , '' replied the Intendant , `` and you have probably saved me ; at all events you have warded off all suspicion , and those who are spies upon me will now have nothing to report except to my favour .
Parsing [sent. 4754 len. 21]: Your absence has been commented upon , and made known at high quarters , and suspicion has arisen in consequence .
Parsing [sent. 4755 len. 18]: Your return as one of the Parliamentary forces will now put an end to all ill-natured remarks .
Parsing [sent. 4756 len. 11]: My dear Edward , you have done me a service .
Parsing [sent. 4757 len. 49]: As my secretary , and having been known to have been a follower of the Beverleys , your absence was considered strange , and it was intimated at high quarters that you had gone to join the king 's forces , and that with my knowledge and consent .
Parsing [sent. 4758 len. 27]: This I have from Langton ; and it has in consequence injured me not a little : but now your appearance will make all right again .

Parsing [sent. 4770 len. 79]: The Intendant said , as he concluded -- `` Edward , you must now perceive that , for the present , nothing more can be done ; if it pleases the Lord , the time will come when the monarch will be reseated on his throne ; at present , we must bow to the powers that be ; and I tell you frankly it is my opinion that Cromwell aims at sovereignty , and will obtain it .
Parsing [sent. 4771 len. 65]: Perhaps it may be better that we should suffer the infliction for a time , as for a time only can it be upheld , and it may be the cause of the king being more schooled and more fitted to reign than , by what you have told me in the course of your narrative , he at present appears to be . ''
Parsing [sent. 4772 len. 10]: `` Perhaps so , sir , '' replied Edward .

Parsing [sent. 4774 len. 25]: I have seen but little true chivalric feeling , and much of interested motives , in those who have joined the king 's forces .
Parsing [sent. 4775 len. 31]: The army collected was composed of most discordant elements , and were so discontented , so full of jealousy and ill-will , that I am not surprised at the result .
Parsing [sent. 4776 len. 47]: One thing is certain , that there must be a much better feeling existing between all parties , before such a man as Cromwell can ever be moved from his position ; and , for the present , the cause may be considered as lost . ''

Parsing [sent. 4778 len. 37]: You have now seen enough to have subdued that fiery zeal for the cause which previously occupied your whole thoughts ; now let us be prudent , and try if we can not be happy . ''
Parsing [sent. 4779 len. 4]: CHAPTER TWENTY FOUR .

Parsing [sent. 4934 len. 4]: CHAPTER TWENTY FIVE .
Parsing [sent. 4935 len. 43]: For several days Edward remained at home , anxiously awaiting every news which arrived ; expecting every time that the capture of the king would be announced , and , with great joy , finding that hitherto all efforts had been unsuccessful .
Parsing [sent. 4936 len. 22]: But there was a question which now arose in Edward 's mind , and which was the cause of deep reflection .
Parsing [sent. 4937 len. 31]: Since the proposal of sending his sisters away had been started , he felt the great inconvenience of his still representing himself to the Intendant as the grandson of Armitage .
Parsing [sent. 4938 len. 83]: His sisters , if sent to the ladies at Portlake , must be sent without the knowledge of the Intendant ; and if so , the discovery of their absence would soon take place , as Patience Heatherstone would be constantly going over to the cottage ; and he now asked himself the question whether , after all the kindness and confidence which the Intendant had shown him , he was right in any longer concealing from him his birth and parentage .
Parsing [sent. 4939 len. 21]: He felt that he was doing the Intendant an injustice in not showing to him that confidence which he deserved .
Parsing [sent. 4940 len. 53]: That he was justified in so doing at first , he felt ; but since the joining the king 's army , and the events which had followed , he considered that he was treating the Intendant ill , and he now resolved to take the first opportunity of making the confession .
Parsing [sent. 4941 len. 18]: But to do it formally , and without some opportunity which might offer , he felt awkward .
Parsing [sent. 4942 len. 21]: At last he thought that he would at once make the confession to Patience , under the promise of secrecy .
